Two Proofs That Al-Ta'ayyun Is a Mental Consideration
Two additional proofs that al-ta'ayyun has no reality in external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs demonstrating that al-ta'ayyun (determination) is merely a mental concept with no external reality.

Core Idea
Al-ta'ayyun cannot exist externally without leading to regress or contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents two compelling proofs that al-ta'ayyun is a purely mental consideration. The first proof argues that if ta'ayyun existed externally, it would itself require another ta'ayyun, leading to an infinite regress. The second proof shows that the essence of ta'ayyun is universal, and distinction requires accidental properties that cannot belong to ta'ayyun in external existence. These arguments are fundamental to understanding the nature of existence and mental concepts in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: لوجهين introduces the two faces.
2
Identify the first proof and its objection
The first proof is based on regress: if ta'ayyun existed externally, it would need a ta'ayyun itself. The objection claims ta'ayyuns are distinct by themselves. Note how the author answers using the necessity of universal essences.
The answer introduces the concept of universal essence in the intellect.
3
Identify the second proof
The second proof emerges from the objection that ta'ayyun's ta'ayyun could be itself. The author argues that ta'ayyun's essence is universal, so distinction requires accidental properties, which are distinct from the subject in external existence.
The examples of 'qidam of the qadim' and 'huduth of the hadith' illustrate mental unification.
4
Understand the overall conclusion
Reflect on how both proofs together establish that ta'ayyun is a mental concept. The first shows external existence leads to regress; the second shows it leads to contradiction in the nature of essence and accident.
This reinforces the doctrine that determinations exist only in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان لوجهين : - الأول: أنه لو كان موجوداً في الخارج لكان له تعين ضرورة (٢) ، وينقل الكلام إليه ويتسلسل . فإن قيل : لا نسلم أنه لو كان موجوداً لكان له تعين، وإنما يلزم ذلك (٣) لو كانت التعينات متشاركة في الماهية ، ليحتاج في التمايز إلى تعين، وهو ممنوع بل هي متخالفة بالماهية متمايزة بالذات، وإنما يتشارك في لفظ التعين أو في عرض لها هو مفهوم التعين . قلنا : ضروري إن لكل موجود ماهية كلية في العقل، وإن امتنع تعدد أفرادها بحسب الخارج، وهذا في حق الواجب محل نظر، فلذا خص الدعوى بالتعين، وإن كانت المناقشة باقية . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون تعين التعين نفسه لا زائداً عليه ليتسلسل : قلنا : لأن ماهية التعين كلية ، وإنما التمايز بالخصوصيات العارضة التي لا تقبل الاشتراك، وتغاير المعروض والعارض في الأمور الموجودة في الخارج ضروري، وإنما يصح الاتحاد وبحسب الواقع في الأمور الاعتبارية كقدم القدم وحدوث الحدوث (٤) . قال : - (٢) في (أ) بزيادة (ضرورة) . (٣) سقط من (ب) لفظ (ذلك ) . (٤) في (ب) كقدم القديم، وحدوث الحادث.
A mental consideration with no realization in external existents for two faces: The first: that if it existed externally, it would have a ta'ayyun necessarily (2), and the discourse would be transferred to it and lead to regress. If it is said: we do not concede that if it existed it would have a ta'ayyun; rather, that would be necessary (3) only if the ta'ayyuns shared in essence, so they would need distinction by ta'ayyun, which is denied; rather they are different in essence, distinct by themselves, and they only share in the word 'ta'ayyun' or in an accident that is the concept of ta'ayyun. We say: it is necessary that every existent has a universal essence in the intellect, even if its individuals are impossible externally, and this regarding the Necessary is a point of consideration, hence the claim was specifically about ta'ayyun, although the objection remains. If it is said: why is it not permissible that the ta'ayyun of ta'ayyun is itself, not additional to it, to avoid regress? We say: because the essence of ta'ayyun is universal, and distinction is only by accidental properties that do not admit commonality, and the distinction between subject and accident in existent things externally is necessary, and unification according to reality is only valid in mental matters, like the qidam of al-qadim and the huduth of al-hadith (4). He said: - (2) In (A) there is an addition of 'ضرورة'. (3) The word 'ذلك' is dropped from (B). (4) In (B): like qidam al-qadim and huduth al-hadith.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage presents two further proofs that al-ta'ayyun is a mental consideration with no reality in external existents. The first proof argues that if ta'ayyun existed externally, it would have a ta'ayyun itself, leading to transfer of discourse and regress. An objection that ta'ayyun is distinct by itself is answered by stating that every existent has a universal essence in the intellect, and the objection is specifically about the Necessary, so the discussion is about ta'ayyun in general. A further objection that the ta'ayyun of ta'ayyun could be itself is answered by showing that the essence of ta'ayyun is universal, and distinction requires accidental properties; thus, subject and accident must be distinct in external things, while unity is only possible in mental matters, as in the examples of the 'qidam of the qadim' and 'huduth of the hadith'. The passage ends with textual variant notes.

تحقق
عين
ضرورة
لفظ
عرض
ماهية كلية
خصوصية عارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the essence of ta'ayyun is particular and self-referential, avoiding regress.
Correction: The essence of ta'ayyun is universal, so it cannot be self-predicated; distinction requires accidental properties, which are distinct from the subject in external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would happen if ta'ayyun existed externally?
A) It would be self-sufficient
B) It would have a ta'ayyun and lead to regress
C) It would be identical to its subject
D) It would be a universal concept

Key Takeaway
The two proofs establish that al-ta'ayyun is a mental concept because external existence would lead to regress or require impossible self-predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you conceptualize Allah's attributes, do you unconsciously treat each attribute as a separate 'determination' (ta'ayyun) in external reality? This lesson shows such thinking leads to regress. How will you train your mind to see them as mental distinctions while affirming they are rooted in one undivided essence?
?
Consider your own self: you often describe yourself with qualities like 'kind' or 'patient'. Are these accidental properties (خصوصيات عارضة) distinct from your essence, or do you mistakenly assume they are your very identity? According to this lesson, in external existence the subject and accident are necessarily distinct. How does this affect your understanding of your own being before Allah?

First Proof: Determination is Conceptual
First proof that determination is conceptual, with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first dialectical proof that determination (تعين) is a conceptual matter, not a real existent, and how the author defends it against objections.

Core Idea
Determination cannot be real without leading to infinite regress.

This passage presents the first dialectical argument that determination (تعين) is a conceptual (اعتباري) rather than a real attribute. The author lays out the main proof, anticipates objections, and provides replies, establishing that determination must be a mental construct, not an external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the main proof and first objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. The argument begins with an objection that when a quiddity exists, it is already particularized, making determination redundant. Note the author's reply about conceptual priority.
The author first states that determination is conceptual with two reasons; the first reason is the focus here.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
The first reason: if determination existed externally, it would need another determination, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل). Grasp why this forces determination to be conceptual.
3
Examine the objection about quiddity diversity
The opponent objects that determinations might be diverse in quiddity and thus not need a further determination. The author replies that every existent has a universal quiddity in the mind, even if external multiplicity is impossible.
This objection is denied; the author restricts the argument to contingent beings because the Necessary Existent is a special case.
4
Consider the second objection about self-determination
Another objection: maybe the determination of determination is itself, avoiding regress. The author replies that determination's quiddity is universal, and distinction requires particularizing accidents that are not sharable.
The footnote clarifies that the priority in the reply is essential (بالذات), not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الماهية إذا وجدت وجدت متخصصة معروضة للتعين لا انهما يتحققان، فيتقارنان ليلزم تميز سابق .
قلنا : تقدم المعروض بالوجود المقارن للتميز ضروري وفيه نظر ) (۱) .
أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان لوجهين : - الأول : أنه لو كان موجوداً لكان له تعين ضرورة (٢)، وينقل الكلام إليه ويتسلسل .
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أنه لو كان موجوداً لكان له تعين، وإنما يلزم ذلك (۳) لو كانت التعينات متشاركة في الماهية ، ليحتاج في التمايز إلى تعين، وهو ممنوع بل هي متخالفة بالماهية متمايزة بالذات، وإنما يتشارك في لفظ التعين أو في عرض لها هو مفهوم التعين .
قلنا ضروري إن لكل موجود ماهية كلية في العقل، وإن امتنع تعدد أفرادها بحسب الخارج، وهذا في حق الواجب محل نظر، فلذا خص الدعوى بالتعين، وإن كانت المناقشة باقية .
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون تعين التعين نفسه لا زائداً عليه ليتسلسل :
قلنا : لأن ماهية التعين كلية ، وإنما التمايز بالخصوصيات العارضة التي لا تقبل الاشتراك، وتغاير المعروض والعارض في الأمور الموجودة في الخارج ضروري، وإنما يصح الاتحاد وبحسب الواقع في الأمور الاعتبارية كقدم القدم وحدوث الحدوث (٤) .
قال : -
(۱) لأن تقدم المعروض على العارض لا نسلم أنه بالزمان حتى يلزم ما ذكر بل بالذات بمعنى أن المعروض من حيث احتياج العارض إليه لكونه وصفاً والمعروض موصوف يلاحظه الذهن بالتقدم الذاتي ليسند إليه العارض وإما ان يتقدم عليه بالزمان فلا يجب بل يجوز أن يفارقه .
(۲) في (أ) بزيادة (ضرورة) .
(۳) سقط من (ب) لفظ (ذلك ) .
(٤) في (ب) كقدم القديم، وحدوث الحادث.
If it is said: The quiddity, when it exists, exists as particularized, being a substrate for determination — not that they both come into existence together, so they would be concomitant, necessitating a prior distinction. We say: The priority of the substrate by the existence that is concurrent with distinction is necessary, but it is questionable (1). It is a conceptual matter having no realization in external existents, for two reasons: The first: That if it existed externally, it would necessarily have a determination (2), and the discussion would transfer to it, leading to infinite regress. If it is said: We do not concede that if it existed it would have a determination; rather that would only be necessary (3) if determinations shared in quiddity, so that distinction would require a determination — which is denied; rather they are diverse in quiddity, distinct by essence, and only share in the word 'determination' or in an accident of them which is the concept of determination. We say: It is necessary that every existent has a universal quiddity in the mind, even if the multiplicity of its individuals externally is impossible — and this is questionable in the case of the Necessary, so the claim is restricted to determination, even if the objection remains. If it is said: Why is it not possible that the determination of determination is itself, not additional to it, to avoid infinite regress? We say: Because the quiddity of determination is universal, and distinction is only through particularizing accidents that do not admit of sharing, and the differentiation of substrate and accident in things existing externally is necessary; unification in accordance with reality is only valid in conceptual matters, like the eternity of the Eternal and the temporality of the temporal (4). He said: - (1) Because the priority of the substrate over the accident — we do not concede that it is temporal such that what was mentioned would follow; rather it is essential priority, meaning that the substrate, insofar as the accident needs it because the accident is a description and the substrate is described, the mind observes it as essentially prior to attribute the accident to it; but it is not necessary that it be prior temporally; rather it may separate from it. (2) In (A): with an addition of (ضرورة). (3) In (B): the word (ذلك) is omitted. (4) In (B): like the eternity of the Eternal, and the temporality of the temporal.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument proving that determination (تعين) is a conceptual (اعتباري) rather than real existent. It begins with an objection: when a quiddity (ماهية) exists, it exists already particularized and is a substrate for determination, but determination is not something that comes into existence along with it; rather they are concomitant, implying a prior distinction. The author answers that the priority of the substrate over the accident is necessary but only conceptually—this is then noted as questionable. He then states the first reason: if determination were real, it would itself require a determination, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل). An objection follows: maybe determinations do not share a quiddity, so each is distinct by itself without needing a further determination. The author replies that every existent has a universal quiddity in the mind, even if multiple individuals are not externally possible—but this reasoning is problematic for the Necessary Existent, so the argument is restricted to contingent determinations. Another objection: perhaps the determination of a determination is the same as the determination itself, avoiding regress. The author answers that determination's quiddity is universal, and distinction requires particularizing accidents that are not sharable; the difference between substrate and accident is necessary in real things, and identity only holds for conceptual matters, like the eternity of the Eternal (a conceptual identity). The passage then includes a footnote clarifying that the priority in question is essential (بالذات) not temporal (بالزمان), and variant readings from different manuscripts.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That determination is a real attribute of things, existing externally as a distinct entity.
Correction: The author argues that determination is conceptual (اعتباري) because if it were real, it would require another determination ad infinitum. Additionally, objections that determinations might be self-distinct or self-determined are answered by showing the universality of quiddity and the need for particularizing accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why can't determination be a real existent?
A) Because it would need another determination, leading to infinite regress.
B) Because determination is a universal concept that applies to all things.
C) Because determination is the same as the quiddity of a thing.

Key Takeaway
Determination is a conceptual matter because if it were a real existent, it would require another determination, leading to infinite regress; the objections are answered by clarifying the nature of quiddity and the necessity of particularizing accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that determination is merely conceptual, not a real existent. Consider a time when you treated a label or category (like 'student', 'parent', 'success') as if it were a real, fixed thing. How did that attachment affect your actions and your relationship with Allah? Will you now see such determinations as mere concepts, freeing you to focus on what is truly real?
?
The argument against infinite regress shows that reliance on conceptual constructs can lead to endless reasoning. Reflect on a worry or anxiety that you have perpetuated by repeatedly seeking a certain outcome (a 'determination') that has no ultimate reality. How can you break this cycle by affirming that only Allah is truly real and that all else are temporary, conceptual distinctions?
?

In your life, do you sometimes impose conceptual determinations on Allah (e.g., expecting Him to act in a certain way based on your concepts)? How can you ensure that your understanding of Allah is free from such conceptual limitations, acknowledging that His essence is beyond determination?

Second Proof: Circularity If Determination Were Real
Second proof that determination is conceptual, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that determination (تعين) is conceptual, including the objection that uses differentiae and the author's resolution distinguishing essential from temporal priority.

Core Idea
If determination were real, it would trap us in circularity or infinite regress.

This passage presents the second proof that determination is merely conceptual, not an extra real attribute. The proof is sharp: if determination were a real existent, it would lead to a logical dead end — either circularity or an infinite chain. An objector tries to escape by comparing to differentiate, but the author argues back by distinguishing essential priority from temporal priority. This lesson unpacks the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the English translation. Notice the logical moves: proof, clarification, objection, answer, further objection, final answer.
The footnote markers are OCR artifacts; ignore them.
2
Identify the core proof
Find the statement that if determination were external, it would lead to circularity (دور) or infinite regress (تسلسل). Understand why: because the instance must be distinct before determination can attach, but if distinction is by determination itself, that's circular; if by another determination, regress.
This is the heart of the argument.
3
Examine the objection and its analogy
The objector asks: why can't the substrate (the instance) already be distinct by this very determination, like a white body being white by its whiteness? Understand the parallel they are drawing.
The objector is trying to break the circularity by making determination self-grounding.
4
Follow the author's answer and refinement
The author replies that existence precedes the accident, so distinction is prior to determination. Then he clarifies that this is essential (not temporal) priority, so the objection about differentiae (which are intellectually distinct) does not apply. The final exchange about 'thisness' shows that the substrate's priority does not prevent its thisness being by determination.
Note the distinction between essential and temporal priority — a key kalam tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : وقد يستدل أي على كون (۱) التعين اعتبارياً بأنه لو وجد في الخارج لتوقف عروضه لحصة هذا الشخص من النوع دون الحصة الأخرى منه على وجودها وتميزها، فإن كان تميزها بهذا التعين فدور أو بتعين آخر فيتسلسل، وهذا هو المراد بقولهم : لو وجد لتوقف انضمامه إلى الماهية على تميزها، فلا يرد ما قيل أن تميز الماهية بذاتها، وبما لها من الفصول، لا بهذا التعين .
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون هذا (1) المعروض هو الحصة المتميزة بهذا التعين لا بتعين سابق؟ ليلزم المحال، كما أن معروض البياض، هو الجسم الأبيض به (۳) لا بياض آخر . وحاصله أن ذلك دور معية، فإن الماهية إذا وجدت، وجدت متخصصة متميزة بما عرضت له من التعينات كحصص الأنواع من الجنس تتمايز بالفصول، ولا يتوقف اختصاص كل (٤) فصل بحصة على تميز لها سابق.
قلنا : وجود المعروض متقدم على العارض بالضرورة، فكذا تميزه لكونه مقارناً للوجود السابق، وهذا بخلاف الفصول، وحصص الأنواع من الجنس، فإن التمايز هناك عقلي لا غير وفيه نظر لأن تقدم معروض التعين عليه، إنما هو بالذات دون الزمان وهو ما يستلزم تقدم ما معه بالزمان، لجواز أن يكون الشيء محتاجاً إليه، ولا يكون مقارنه كذلك .
فإن قيل : المعروض المتقدم هو هذه الحصة، فيلزم تقدم الهذية(ه)، وهو التعين والتميز.
قلنا : نعم بمعنى أنه معروض الهذية فلا يمتنع أن يكون هذيتها بهذا التعين.
The second: And it may be argued, i.e., that determination is conceptual, because if it existed externally, its occurrence to this particular instance of the species — and not to another instance — would depend on that instance's existence and distinction. If its distinction is by this determination, then there is circularity (دور), or if by another determination, then there is infinite regress (تسلسل). This is what is meant by their statement: If it existed, its attachment to the quiddity would depend on its distinction. So the objection that the quiddity is distinct by itself and by its differentiate, not by this determination, is not valid. If it is said: Why is it not possible that this (1) substrate is the particular instance distinct by this determination, not by a prior determination, so that the impossible does not follow, just as the substrate of whiteness is the white body by it (3), not by another whiteness. In sum, that is a circularity of concomitance (دور معية), for the quiddity, when it exists, exists as particularized and distinct by the determinations that occur to it, like the particular instances of species from a genus that are distinguished by differentiate, and the specification of each (4) differentia to an instance does not depend on a prior distinction for it. We say: The existence of the substrate is necessarily prior to the accident, and likewise its distinction, because it is concurrent with the prior existence. This is unlike the differentiate and the particular instances of species from a genus, for the distinction there is merely intellectual — and there is consideration in it, because the priority of the substrate of determination over it is only essential, not temporal, and that does not necessitate the temporal priority of what accompanies it, for it is possible that a thing needs something while its concomitant does not need it in the same way. If it is said: The prior substrate is this particular instance, so it requires the priority of the 'thisness' (الهذية), which is determination and distinction. We say: Yes, meaning it is the substrate of thisness, so it is not impossible that its thisness be by this determination.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is the second proof that determination is conceptual, followed by an objection and answer. The proof states that if determination were a real existent, then its occurrence to a specific instance of a species would require that instance to already exist and be distinct. If that instance's distinction comes from this determination, that is circular (دور); if it comes from another determination, that leads to infinite regress (تسلسل). So determination cannot be real. The author notes that this refutes the counterclaim that quiddities are distinct by themselves or by their differentiae. An objection is then raised: why not say the substrate is the instance that is already distinct by this determination itself, not by a prior one, similar to whiteness where the substrate is the white body by that whiteness? The objector argues that the quiddity exists already distinct by the determinations that occur to it, like species instances distinguished by differentiae without requiring prior distinction. The author answers that existence of the substrate is prior to the accident, so its distinction (as concurrent with existence) is also prior. However, this is unlike differentiate and instances, where distinction is intellectual (عقلي) not real. The author then notes a consideration: the priority of the substrate over determination is essential (بالذات), not temporal, so it does not force temporal priority of the concomitant. A further objection says: if the prior substrate is the particular instance itself, then its 'thisness' (هذية) — which is determination and distinction — is prior. The author agrees, but explains that 'thisness' being prior only means the substrate is the subject of thisness, and it is not impossible that the thisness is by this determination.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quiddity's distinction through differentiate is a good model for how determination could be real without circularity.
Correction: The author shows that distinction by differentiate is merely intellectual (عقلي), not real external distinction. Therefore, the analogy does not save the real existence of determination; the proof stands.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, what are the two unacceptable consequences if determination were a real existent?
A) It would lead to either circularity or infinite regress.
B) Determination would need to be prior to existence.
C) Determination would become a universal concept.
D) It would make quiddities indistinguishable.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that if determination were real, it would create an impossible logical situation (circularity or infinite regress), so it must be conceptual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own identity—your sense of being 'you'—do you treat it as a fixed, real thing? This lesson shows that determination (individuality) is merely conceptual, not externally real. Are you willing to see your self-image as a mental construct, and what would that mean for your attachment to it?
?
The argument uses circularity and infinite regress to expose an impossibility. Think about your own reasoning: are there beliefs you hold that rely on circular justification? Will you commit to honestly tracing your arguments back to first principles, even if it means abandoning comfortable assumptions?
?
The objector's analogy with whiteness sounds plausible at first, but the author shows it fails because of a deeper principle. In your life, do you ever accept analogies or comparisons without checking their logical foundation? This lesson demands rigorous thinking—will you apply that same rigor to your own theological and philosophical positions?

First Argument: Ta'ayyun as Part of the Determinate
Refutation of the first argument for ta'ayyun's reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's first argument that ta'ayyun must be existent because it is part of the determinate thing, and the refutation that distinguishes between the substrate and the accident, showing that the real existent is the individual restricted by accidents, not a composite including ta'ayyun.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun is not a part of the existent; the existent is the substrate of ta'ayyun, not the composite.

The opponent's first argument attempts to prove ta'ayyun is a real existent by claiming it is a constituent part of the determinate individual (muta'ayyan). Since the individual exists, its part must exist. This lesson unpacks the argument and the nuanced refutation that distinguishes between the underlying substrate and the accidental determination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow of objection and response.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that ta'ayyun is part of the determinate thing, and since the determinate exists, ta'ayyun must exist.
3
Analyze the initial refutation
The author responds that the existent is the substrate of ta'ayyun, not the composite of substrate and accident. The composite is i'tibari (mental).
Key distinction: substrate vs. composite.
4
Examine the sub-objection and answer
A sub-objection reframes the determinate as the individual (e.g., Zayd), which includes ta'ayyun as a part. The author answers that the individual is the human restricted by individuating accidents, not a composite of humanity and ta'ayyun.
The real existents are the accidents like quantity, quality, place.
5
Conclude why the argument fails
The argument fails because it conflates the substrate with the composite; ta'ayyun is not an intrinsic part but a relational consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجاج المخالف لمدعي اعتبارية التعين. قال: احتج المخالف بوجوه: الأول: أنه جزء المتعين وهو موجود. قلنا: الموجود معروض التعين لا المركب من المعروض والعارض فإنه اعتباري. فإن قيل: المتعين هو الشخص كزيد مثلاً، ولا خفاء في وجوده، وليس مفهومه مجرد الإنسان، بل مع شيء آخر نسميه التعين، فيكون جزءاً من زيد الموجود فيوجد. فالجواب: أنه الإنسان المقيد بالعوارض المشخصة لا المجموع، ولو سلم فذلك الشيء هو المشخصات من الكم والكيف، والأين المخصوصة ونحو ذلك مما وجوده ضروري، وإنما الكلام في التشخص. الثاني: أن طبيعة النوع الواحد لا تتكثر بنفسها، بل بما ينضاف إليها وهو المراد بالتشخيص. الثالث: لو كان عدمياً لما كان متعيناً في نفسه، فلا تعين غيره. قلنا: غير المتنازع. الرابع: لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً للاتعين مطلقاً، أو لتعين آخر عدمي أو ثبوتي فيكون ثبوتياً لأن رفع العدمي ثبوتي، وحكم الأمثال واحد. قلنا: بعد المساعدة على أن العدمي عدم لشيء، وأن نقيضه ثبوتي إن أريد باللاتعين والتعين مفهوماً فهما لا حصر وما صدقا عليه، فلا يلزم كون ما صدق عليه اللاتعين عدمياً. الخامس: لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً لما ينافيه، فإن كان عدماً للإطلاق، أو لما يساويه كان مشتركاً بين الأفراد كعدم الإطلاق، فلا يكون متميزاً، إن لم يكن لزم جواز انفكاكه عن عدم الإطلاق، إما بتحقق عدم الإطلاق بدونه فيكون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً وإما بالعكس فيكون مطلقاً ومعيناً. قلنا: إن أريد مطلق التعين لم يمتنع اشتراكه بين الأفراد، وتمايزها بالتعينات الخاصة وإن أريد التعين الخاص لم يمتنع كون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً لجواز أن يكون معيناً بتعين آخر.
The opponent's argument against the one who claims ta'ayyun is i'tibari. He said: The opponent argued with points: The first: that it is a part of the muta'ayyan (determinate thing) and it exists. We said: the existent is the substrate of ta'ayyun, not the composite of substrate and accident, for that is i'tibari. If it is said: the muta'ayyan is the individual like Zayd for example, and there is no doubt in its existence, and its concept is not merely human, but rather with something else that we call ta'ayyun, so it becomes a part of the existent Zayd and thus exists. The answer: it is the human restricted by the individuating accidents, not the composite; and if conceded, that thing is the individuators (mushakhkhasāt) from quantity, quality, and the specific where (ayn) and the like, whose existence is necessary, and the discussion is only about tashakhkhus (individuation). The second: that the nature of a single species does not become multiple by itself, but only by what is added to it, and that is what is meant by tashakhkhus. The third: if it were non-existent, it would not be determinate in itself, so it would not determine another. We said: it is beyond dispute. The fourth: if it were non-existent, it would be a non-existence of non-determination absolutely, or of another non-existent or existent determination, and would then be existent because the removal of a non-existent is existent, and the rule of similar things is one. We said: after conceding that the non-existent is a non-existence of something, and that its opposite is existent, if what is meant by non-determination and determination are concepts, then they are not exclusive and what they apply to; so it does not follow that what non-determination applies to is non-existent. The fifth: if it were non-existent, it would be a non-existence of what contradicts it; if it were a non-existence of absoluteness, or of what equals it, it would be common among individuals like the non-existence of absoluteness, so it would not be distinguished; if not, then its separation from non-existence of absoluteness would be possible, either by the realization of non-existence of absoluteness without it, so the thing would be neither absolute nor determinate, or by the opposite, so it would be both absolute and determinate. We said: if what is meant is absolute ta'ayyun, its being common among individuals is not impossible, and their distinction is by specific ta'ayyunāt; if what is meant is specific ta'ayyun, it is not impossible for a thing to be neither absolute nor determinate, because it might be determinate by another ta'ayyun.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage presents a polemical structure: the opponent (المخالف) brings five arguments to prove that ta'ayyun (determination/individualization) is a real, existent entity, not merely a mental consideration (i'tibari). The author (the teacher) refutes each argument in turn. The first argument claims that ta'ayyun is a part of the determinate thing (المتعين) and since the determinate thing exists, its part exists. The refutation distinguishes: the existent is the substrate (معروض) of ta'ayyun, not the composite of substrate and accident; the composite is i'tibari. A sub-objection says the determinate individual (e.g., Zayd) includes ta'ayyun as a part. The answer: Zayd is the human restricted by individuating accidents, not the sum of humanity and something else; if conceded, that something else refers to real accidents like quantity, quality, and place, which are indeed existent, but the dispute is about 'tashakhkhus' itself, not those accidents. The second argument: a species' nature does not become multiple by itself; it needs an added factor, which is tashakhkhus. This is presented without an explicit refutation in the text, but it is implied that this added factor is not necessarily a real entity. The third argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent (ʿadami), it could not be determinate in itself and thus could not determine another. The refutation is brief: 'non-disputed' (غير المتنازع), meaning the opponent's premise that ta'ayyun would need to be determinate in itself is not conceded. The fourth argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be either the non-existence of non-determination absolutely or of another determination, leading to a regress or to it being existent because removing a non-existent is existent. The refutation: after conceding the logical point about opposites, if 'non-determination' and 'determination' are taken as concepts (مفهوم), they are not exclusive extensions; therefore, what non-determination applies to (i.e., the thing itself) is not necessarily non-existent. The fifth argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be the non-existence of what contradicts it (i.e., absoluteness); if it is the non-existence of absoluteness, it would be common to all individuals and not distinguishing; if not, then it could separate from non-existence of absoluteness, leading to contradictions (neither absolute nor determinate, or both). The refutation distinguishes: if by ta'ayyun is meant absolute determination, its being common is not impossible; individuals are distinguished by specific determinations. If specific determination is meant, a thing can be neither absolute nor determinate because it might have another determination. In all cases, the opponent's arguments fail to prove that ta'ayyun is an existent entity; rather, it is a relational or mental consideration.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun)
متعين (muta'ayyan)
معروض (substrate)
عارض (accident)
اعتباري (i'tibari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'ayyun must be a real existent because it appears to be part of a determinate individual.
Correction: The determinate individual exists as a substrate with real accidents; ta'ayyun is not a separate part but the relation of being determined by those accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the author's initial refutation, what is the existent?
A) The composite of substrate and accident
B) The substrate (ma'rud) of ta'ayyun
C) The accident of ta'ayyun
D) The part called ta'ayyun

Key Takeaway
The first argument fails because it conflates the substrate with the composite; ta'ayyun is not an intrinsic part but a relational consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your individuality (ta'ayyun) is not a separate reality but merely a mental consideration, what does that reveal about your need for Allah to sustain your very identity? Are you clinging to a sense of self that is ultimately contingent?
?
The argument shows that the composite of substrate and accident is i'tibari. In your daily life, do you treat your attributes (e.g., your status, your achievements) as if they are part of you, when in reality they are accidents on a substrate? How can you detach from them?
?
If the true self is the substrate, not the accidental qualities, then what remains of you after stripping away all accidents? Does this thought make you turn to the Eternal, who alone has necessary existence?

Second and Third Arguments: Multiplicity of Nature and Non-Existent Determination
Refutation of the second and third arguments for ta'ayyun's reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two brief arguments: (1) that the multiplicity of a species requires an added factor (tashakhkhus), and (2) that a non-existent ta'ayyun could not determine another; and the author's refutation that these do not prove ta'ayyun's reality.

Core Idea
A species' multiplicity requires an added factor, but that factor need not be a real entity; and a non-existent can still determine mentally.

The opponent's second and third arguments are terse. The second appeals to the principle that a species' nature becomes multiple only through an added factor, which is tashakhkhus. The third argues that if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it could not determine another. This lesson examines these arguments and the author's concise refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the two arguments
Read the Arabic and translation for both arguments. Note their brevity.
2
Analyze the second argument
The opponent says a species' nature becomes multiple only by an added factor (tashakhkhus). This implies tashakhkhus is real. The refutation is implied: the added factor could be mental or relational.
3
Analyze the third argument
The opponent claims if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it could not be determinate itself and thus could not determine another. The author simply says 'non-disputed' (ghayr al-mutanāzi‘), meaning the premise is not conceded.
The opponent assumes a non-existent cannot be determinate, but that assumption is not granted.
4
Understand the refutation style
The author's refutation for the third argument is abrupt, dismissing the argument's foundation. This shows that not all arguments require detailed rebuttal.
5
Conclude the lesson
Neither argument succeeds in proving ta'ayyun is a real entity. The second argument does not necessitate a real added factor, and the third argument rests on an unaccepted premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجاج المخالف لمدعي اعتبارية التعين. قال: احتج المخالف بوجوه: الأول: أنه جزء المتعين وهو موجود. قلنا: الموجود معروض التعين لا المركب من المعروض والعارض فإنه اعتباري. فإن قيل: المتعين هو الشخص كزيد مثلاً، ولا خفاء في وجوده، وليس مفهومه مجرد الإنسان، بل مع شيء آخر نسميه التعين، فيكون جزءاً من زيد الموجود فيوجد. فالجواب: أنه الإنسان المقيد بالعوارض المشخصة لا المجموع، ولو سلم فذلك الشيء هو المشخصات من الكم والكيف، والأين المخصوصة ونحو ذلك مما وجوده ضروري، وإنما الكلام في التشخص. الثاني: أن طبيعة النوع الواحد لا تتكثر بنفسها، بل بما ينضاف إليها وهو المراد بالتشخيص. الثالث: لو كان عدمياً لما كان متعيناً في نفسه، فلا تعين غيره. قلنا: غير المتنازع. الرابع: لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً للاتعين مطلقاً، أو لتعين آخر عدمي أو ثبوتي فيكون ثبوتياً لأن رفع العدمي ثبوتي، وحكم الأمثال واحد. قلنا: بعد المساعدة على أن العدمي عدم لشيء، وأن نقيضه ثبوتي إن أريد باللاتعين والتعين مفهوماً فهما لا حصر وما صدقا عليه، فلا يلزم كون ما صدق عليه اللاتعين عدمياً. الخامس: لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً لما ينافيه، فإن كان عدماً للإطلاق، أو لما يساويه كان مشتركاً بين الأفراد كعدم الإطلاق، فلا يكون متميزاً، إن لم يكن لزم جواز انفكاكه عن عدم الإطلاق، إما بتحقق عدم الإطلاق بدونه فيكون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً وإما بالعكس فيكون مطلقاً ومعيناً. قلنا: إن أريد مطلق التعين لم يمتنع اشتراكه بين الأفراد، وتمايزها بالتعينات الخاصة وإن أريد التعين الخاص لم يمتنع كون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً لجواز أن يكون معيناً بتعين آخر.
The opponent's argument against the one who claims ta'ayyun is i'tibari. He said: The opponent argued with points: The first: that it is a part of the muta'ayyan (determinate thing) and it exists. We said: the existent is the substrate of ta'ayyun, not the composite of substrate and accident, for that is i'tibari. If it is said: the muta'ayyan is the individual like Zayd for example, and there is no doubt in its existence, and its concept is not merely human, but rather with something else that we call ta'ayyun, so it becomes a part of the existent Zayd and thus exists. The answer: it is the human restricted by the individuating accidents, not the composite; and if conceded, that thing is the individuators (mushakhkhasāt) from quantity, quality, and the specific where (ayn) and the like, whose existence is necessary, and the discussion is only about tashakhkhus (individuation). The second: that the nature of a single species does not become multiple by itself, but only by what is added to it, and that is what is meant by tashakhkhus. The third: if it were non-existent, it would not be determinate in itself, so it would not determine another. We said: it is beyond dispute. The fourth: if it were non-existent, it would be a non-existence of non-determination absolutely, or of another non-existent or existent determination, and would then be existent because the removal of a non-existent is existent, and the rule of similar things is one. We said: after conceding that the non-existent is a non-existence of something, and that its opposite is existent, if what is meant by non-determination and determination are concepts, then they are not exclusive and what they apply to; so it does not follow that what non-determination applies to is non-existent. The fifth: if it were non-existent, it would be a non-existence of what contradicts it; if it were a non-existence of absoluteness, or of what equals it, it would be common among individuals like the non-existence of absoluteness, so it would not be distinguished; if not, then its separation from non-existence of absoluteness would be possible, either by the realization of non-existence of absoluteness without it, so the thing would be neither absolute nor determinate, or by the opposite, so it would be both absolute and determinate. We said: if what is meant is absolute ta'ayyun, its being common among individuals is not impossible, and their distinction is by specific ta'ayyunāt; if what is meant is specific ta'ayyun, it is not impossible for a thing to be neither absolute nor determinate, because it might be determinate by another ta'ayyun.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage presents a polemical structure: the opponent (المخالف) brings five arguments to prove that ta'ayyun (determination/individualization) is a real, existent entity, not merely a mental consideration (i'tibari). The author (the teacher) refutes each argument in turn. The first argument claims that ta'ayyun is a part of the determinate thing (المتعين) and since the determinate thing exists, its part exists. The refutation distinguishes: the existent is the substrate (معروض) of ta'ayyun, not the composite of substrate and accident; the composite is i'tibari. A sub-objection says the determinate individual (e.g., Zayd) includes ta'ayyun as a part. The answer: Zayd is the human restricted by individuating accidents, not the sum of humanity and something else; if conceded, that something else refers to real accidents like quantity, quality, and place, which are indeed existent, but the dispute is about 'tashakhkhus' itself, not those accidents. The second argument: a species' nature does not become multiple by itself; it needs an added factor, which is tashakhkhus. This is presented without an explicit refutation in the text, but it is implied that this added factor is not necessarily a real entity. The third argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent (ʿadami), it could not be determinate in itself and thus could not determine another. The refutation is brief: 'non-disputed' (غير المتنازع), meaning the opponent's premise that ta'ayyun would need to be determinate in itself is not conceded. The fourth argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be either the non-existence of non-determination absolutely or of another determination, leading to a regress or to it being existent because removing a non-existent is existent. The refutation: after conceding the logical point about opposites, if 'non-determination' and 'determination' are taken as concepts (مفهوم), they are not exclusive extensions; therefore, what non-determination applies to (i.e., the thing itself) is not necessarily non-existent. The fifth argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be the non-existence of what contradicts it (i.e., absoluteness); if it is the non-existence of absoluteness, it would be common to all individuals and not distinguishing; if not, then it could separate from non-existence of absoluteness, leading to contradictions (neither absolute nor determinate, or both). The refutation distinguishes: if by ta'ayyun is meant absolute determination, its being common is not impossible; individuals are distinguished by specific determinations. If specific determination is meant, a thing can be neither absolute nor determinate because it might have another determination. In all cases, the opponent's arguments fail to prove that ta'ayyun is an existent entity; rather, it is a relational or mental consideration.

Key Terms
تشخص (tashakhkhus)
طبيعة (nature)
تكثر (multiplicity)
عدم (non-existence)
متعين (determinate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the third argument?
A) By distinguishing substrate from composite
B) By saying it is 'beyond dispute' (ghayr al-mutanāzi‘)
C) By providing a detailed logical analysis
D) By conceding the point

Key Takeaway
The second argument does not force ta'ayyun to be real; the third argument rests on an unaccepted premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is necessary for a outcome, it must be real in the strongest sense? What in your life do you treat as real simply because it has effects, and are you willing to accept it might be a mere mental concept?
?
When you reflect on your own identity—what makes you 'you'—do you instinctively imagine a real 'thing' inside? Could you accept that the principle of your individuality might be a mental or relational notion rather than an extra-mental entity? If so, how does that change your understanding of yourself and your connection to God?
?
The opponent insisted that non-existence cannot be determinate. But can you think of something in your own life that is not physically real yet is 'determinate' in how it shapes you—like a memory, a promise, or a conviction? Will you allow that mental determination is enough, or are you still tempted to demand a physical anchor?

Fourth Argument: Non-Existent Determination and Its Opposite
Refutation of the fourth argument for ta'ayyun's reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's fourth argument that if ta'ayyun were non-existent it would lead to a regress or become existent, and the author's refutation distinguishing between conceptual and extra-mental application.

Core Idea
Non-existence of non-determination is not necessarily non-existent; conceptual application avoids the regress.

The opponent's fourth argument is more complex, attempting to trap the i'tibari view in a dilemma: if ta'ayyun is non-existent, then either it is the non-existence of non-determination absolutely, or it is the non-existence of another determination—both allegedly leading to it being existent. This lesson unpacks the logical moves and the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth argument. Note the structure: a conditional with two branches.
2
Understand the opponent's dilemma
If ta'ayyun is non-existent, it is the non-existence of something. Against non-determination (la ta'ayyun) or against another determination. Both cases lead to it being existent.
The opponent uses the principle 'raf' al-ʿadami thubūtī' (removal of non-existence is existence).
3
Analyze the refutation
The author concedes the logical principle but distinguishes: if 'non-determination' and 'determination' are taken as concepts (mafhūm), they do not apply exclusively to extra-mental things, so the thing that non-determination applies to (i.e., the thing itself) is not necessarily non-existent.
4
See the key distinction
The argument conflates conceptual non-existence with real non-existence. The author saves the i'tibari view by clarifying the domain of discussion.
5
Conclude
The fourth argument fails because it assumes that non-determination is a real attribute, when it can be a mental concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If ta'ayyun is non-existent, it must be the non-existence of something, which leads to a contradiction or its existence.
Correction: The concepts of non-determination and determination are not exclusive in extra-mental reality; a thing can be non-determined in one sense and determined in another.

What is the key move in the author's refutation of the fourth argument?
A) Conceding the argument
B) Distinguishing between concepts and their extra-mental application
C) Denying that removal of non-existence is existence
D) Rejecting the premise that ta'ayyun is non-existent

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because it incorrectly assumes that 'non-determination' is a real attribute, not a mental concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defined yourself by what you are not (a non-determination) and felt less real? This lesson shows that even what you are not is only a concept—it does not diminish your existence. Will you stop letting absences define you?
?
When you encounter a theological dilemma that seems airtight, do you quickly accept a conclusion without questioning the assumptions? The opponent's argument here seemed strong until the author distinguished concept from application. Can you think of a time you accepted a false conclusion about your faith because you didn't examine the underlying terms? How can you train yourself to pause and look for such distinctions?
?
The argument fails because non-determination is a concept, not a real attribute. In your own life, are there 'nothings'—gaps, lacks, or failures—that you treat as real, permanent flaws? This passage challenges you to see them as mental concepts that do not negate your worth or existence. Will you commit to distinguishing between what is real and what is merely conceptual in your self-assessment?

Fifth Argument: Non-Existent Determination and Absoluteness
Refutation of the fifth argument for ta'ayyun's reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's fifth and most complex argument concerning the relationship between ta'ayyun and absoluteness, and the author's refutation distinguishing between absolute and specific ta'ayyun.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun as non-existence of absoluteness leads to absurdities, but the author shows that absolute ta'ayyun can be common and specific ta'ayyun is not contradictory.

The opponent's fifth argument is the most intricate. It claims that if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be the non-existence of its opposite (absoluteness). Depending on whether it equals absoluteness or not, it leads to either undifferentiated commonality or logical contradictions. The author responds by distinguishing between absolute ta'ayyun and specific ta'ayyun, defusing the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. The argument has multiple conditional branches.
2
Understand the opponent's conditional reasoning
If ta'ayyun is non-existent, it is the non-existence of its opposite (absoluteness). If it equals absoluteness in extension, then it is common among individuals (like non-existence of absoluteness) and fails to distinguish. If it does not equal it, then it can separate from non-existence of absoluteness, leading to contradictions (neither absolute nor determinate, or both).
3
Analyze the refutation's first branch
If 'absolute ta'ayyun' is meant, it being common is not problematic; individuals are distinguished by specific ta'ayyunāt (plural of ta'ayyun).
4
Analyze the refutation's second branch
If 'specific ta'ayyun' is meant, the thing can be neither absolute nor determinate if it has another determination. No contradiction.
5
Conclude
The opponent's dilemma is avoided by clarifying the scope of ta'ayyun. The i'tibari view remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجاج المخالف لمدعي اعتبارية التعين. قال: احتج المخالف بوجوه: الأول: أنه جزء المتعين وهو موجود. قلنا: الموجود معروض التعين لا المركب من المعروض والعارض فإنه اعتباري. فإن قيل: المتعين هو الشخص كزيد مثلاً، ولا خفاء في وجوده، وليس مفهومه مجرد الإنسان، بل مع شيء آخر نسميه التعين، فيكون جزءاً من زيد الموجود فيوجد. فالجواب: أنه الإنسان المقيد بالعوارض المشخصة لا المجموع، ولو سلم فذلك الشيء هو المشخصات من الكم والكيف، والأين المخصوصة ونحو ذلك مما وجوده ضروري، وإنما الكلام في التشخص. الثاني: أن طبيعة النوع الواحد لا تتكثر بنفسها، بل بما ينضاف إليها وهو المراد بالتشخيص. الثالث: لو كان عدمياً لما كان متعيناً في نفسه، فلا تعين غيره. قلنا: غير المتنازع. الرابع: لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً للاتعين مطلقاً، أو لتعين آخر عدمي أو ثبوتي فيكون ثبوتياً لأن رفع العدمي ثبوتي، وحكم الأمثال واحد. قلنا: بعد المساعدة على أن العدمي عدم لشيء، وأن نقيضه ثبوتي إن أريد باللاتعين والتعين مفهوماً فهما لا حصر وما صدقا عليه، فلا يلزم كون ما صدق عليه اللاتعين عدمياً. الخامس: لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً لما ينافيه، فإن كان عدماً للإطلاق، أو لما يساويه كان مشتركاً بين الأفراد كعدم الإطلاق، فلا يكون متميزاً، إن لم يكن لزم جواز انفكاكه عن عدم الإطلاق، إما بتحقق عدم الإطلاق بدونه فيكون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً وإما بالعكس فيكون مطلقاً ومعيناً. قلنا: إن أريد مطلق التعين لم يمتنع اشتراكه بين الأفراد، وتمايزها بالتعينات الخاصة وإن أريد التعين الخاص لم يمتنع كون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً لجواز أن يكون معيناً بتعين آخر.
The opponent's argument against the one who claims ta'ayyun is i'tibari. He said: The opponent argued with points: The first: that it is a part of the muta'ayyan (determinate thing) and it exists. We said: the existent is the substrate of ta'ayyun, not the composite of substrate and accident, for that is i'tibari. If it is said: the muta'ayyan is the individual like Zayd for example, and there is no doubt in its existence, and its concept is not merely human, but rather with something else that we call ta'ayyun, so it becomes a part of the existent Zayd and thus exists. The answer: it is the human restricted by the individuating accidents, not the composite; and if conceded, that thing is the individuators (mushakhkhasāt) from quantity, quality, and the specific where (ayn) and the like, whose existence is necessary, and the discussion is only about tashakhkhus (individuation). The second: that the nature of a single species does not become multiple by itself, but only by what is added to it, and that is what is meant by tashakhkhus. The third: if it were non-existent, it would not be determinate in itself, so it would not determine another. We said: it is beyond dispute. The fourth: if it were non-existent, it would be a non-existence of non-determination absolutely, or of another non-existent or existent determination, and would then be existent because the removal of a non-existent is existent, and the rule of similar things is one. We said: after conceding that the non-existent is a non-existence of something, and that its opposite is existent, if what is meant by non-determination and determination are concepts, then they are not exclusive and what they apply to; so it does not follow that what non-determination applies to is non-existent. The fifth: if it were non-existent, it would be a non-existence of what contradicts it; if it were a non-existence of absoluteness, or of what equals it, it would be common among individuals like the non-existence of absoluteness, so it would not be distinguished; if not, then its separation from non-existence of absoluteness would be possible, either by the realization of non-existence of absoluteness without it, so the thing would be neither absolute nor determinate, or by the opposite, so it would be both absolute and determinate. We said: if what is meant is absolute ta'ayyun, its being common among individuals is not impossible, and their distinction is by specific ta'ayyunāt; if what is meant is specific ta'ayyun, it is not impossible for a thing to be neither absolute nor determinate, because it might be determinate by another ta'ayyun.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage presents a polemical structure: the opponent (المخالف) brings five arguments to prove that ta'ayyun (determination/individualization) is a real, existent entity, not merely a mental consideration (i'tibari). The author (the teacher) refutes each argument in turn. The first argument claims that ta'ayyun is a part of the determinate thing (المتعين) and since the determinate thing exists, its part exists. The refutation distinguishes: the existent is the substrate (معروض) of ta'ayyun, not the composite of substrate and accident; the composite is i'tibari. A sub-objection says the determinate individual (e.g., Zayd) includes ta'ayyun as a part. The answer: Zayd is the human restricted by individuating accidents, not the sum of humanity and something else; if conceded, that something else refers to real accidents like quantity, quality, and place, which are indeed existent, but the dispute is about 'tashakhkhus' itself, not those accidents. The second argument: a species' nature does not become multiple by itself; it needs an added factor, which is tashakhkhus. This is presented without an explicit refutation in the text, but it is implied that this added factor is not necessarily a real entity. The third argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent (ʿadami), it could not be determinate in itself and thus could not determine another. The refutation is brief: 'non-disputed' (غير المتنازع), meaning the opponent's premise that ta'ayyun would need to be determinate in itself is not conceded. The fourth argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be either the non-existence of non-determination absolutely or of another determination, leading to a regress or to it being existent because removing a non-existent is existent. The refutation: after conceding the logical point about opposites, if 'non-determination' and 'determination' are taken as concepts (مفهوم), they are not exclusive extensions; therefore, what non-determination applies to (i.e., the thing itself) is not necessarily non-existent. The fifth argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be the non-existence of what contradicts it (i.e., absoluteness); if it is the non-existence of absoluteness, it would be common to all individuals and not distinguishing; if not, then it could separate from non-existence of absoluteness, leading to contradictions (neither absolute nor determinate, or both). The refutation distinguishes: if by ta'ayyun is meant absolute determination, its being common is not impossible; individuals are distinguished by specific determinations. If specific determination is meant, a thing can be neither absolute nor determinate because it might have another determination. In all cases, the opponent's arguments fail to prove that ta'ayyun is an existent entity; rather, it is a relational or mental consideration.

Key Terms
إطلاق (absoluteness)
تعين مطلق (absolute ta'ayyun)
تعين خاص (specific ta'ayyun)
عدم (non-existence)
انفكاك (separation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If ta'ayyun is non-existent, it must lead to logical contradictions about absoluteness.
Correction: The refutation shows that by distinguishing absolute from specific ta'ayyun, the i'tibari view avoids contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the author's refutation, what is the problem with the opponent's assumption that ta'ayyun equals non-existence of absoluteness?
A) It leads to infinite regress
B) It makes ta'ayyun common among individuals, but that is acceptable if absolute ta'ayyun is meant
C) It contradicts the principle of non-contradiction
D) It proves ta'ayyun is existent

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument fails because it does not distinguish between absolute and specific ta'ayyun; the i'tibari view can handle both without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed logically airtight, only to discover that a subtle distinction you overlooked collapsed the entire argument? What was that distinction, and how does this lesson challenge you to be more careful in your own reasoning?
?
This passage shows that two people can argue past each other if they mean different things by the same term. Where in your own life — in discussions with family, friends, or even yourself — are you conflating different meanings of a key concept? What would it cost you to clarify that distinction?
?
The author's refutation depends on the i'tibari nature of ta'ayyun. Consider something in your own faith or practice that you treat as 'real' but might be a matter of i'tibar (consideration). Are you willing to admit that some of your certainties might be based on a mental perspective rather than external reality? How would that change your certainty?

Third Argument Against 'Adamiyyat al-Ta'ayyun and Its Response
Author presents third argument and gives succinct response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that if ta'ayyun were non-existent it would not be determined, and why the author's response ('not the point of dispute') is a valid logical refutation.

Core Idea
An argument against non-existent determination misses the mark if it does not address the actual point of dispute.

In this brief passage, the author presents the third objection to the view that ta'ayyun (determination) is non-existent. The argument claims that if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it could not be determined itself and therefore could not determine anything else. The author's response is famously succinct: 'This is not the point of dispute.' This lesson unpacks why this response is not dismissive but points to a logical flaw in the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the third argument carefully: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would not be determined in itself, so it could not determine another.
Note the logical chain: non-existence → no self-determination → no determination of others.
2
Understand the response
The author says 'We said: This is not the point of dispute.' Ask yourself what the actual point of dispute is, and how this argument fails to engage with it.
The response identifies a mismatch between the argument's premises and the actual disagreement.
3
Analyze the logical disconnect
Consider that the disputants might agree that ta'ayyun is non-existent in some sense, yet the argument's conclusion does not contradict their position because it assumes a definition of 'determined' that is not in contention.
The author's brevity is a sign that the argument is considered weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان عدمياً لما كان متعيناً في نفسه، فلا تعين غيره . قلنا : غير المتنازع .
Third: If it were non-existent, then it would not be determined in itself, so it would not determine another. We said: This is not the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the view that ta'ayyun (determination) is non-existent ('adami). The argument states: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would not be determined in itself, and consequently it could not serve to determine something else. The author ('قلنا' = we said) responds succinctly: 'غير المتنازع' meaning this argument does not address the point of dispute between the two parties. The author implies that the premise that ta'ayyun is determined in itself is not conceded, or that the issue is about something else. The response is brief and dismissive.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun)
عدم ('adam)
متنازع (disputed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's response 'غير المتنازع' is dismissive and avoids the argument.
Correction: The response actually identifies that the argument's premise does not engage with the actual disagreement. The disputants might agree that non-existent things are not 'determined in themselves,' but that is not what is being disputed. The author is pointing out a logical non-sequitur.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the third argument that if ta'ayyun were non-existent it would not be determined?
A) He concedes the point
B) He says it is not the disputed point
C) He argues that ta'ayyun is existent
D) He remains silent

Key Takeaway
The third argument against 'adamiyyat al-ta'ayyun is refuted by pointing out that it does not address the real point of dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author dismisses an argument as 'not the point of dispute.' Have you ever dismissed someone's view because you thought it was logically flawed, only to later realize you misunderstood their claim? How can you cultivate the humility to listen carefully before jumping to refutation?

The Fourth Argument Against 'Adamiyyat al-Ta'ayyun and Its Refutation
The author presents and refutes the fourth argument against 'adamiyyat al-ta'ayyun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that ta'ayyun cannot be non-existent because it would be a negation, and the author's refutation distinguishing conceptual from external existence.

Core Idea
The removal of a non-existent is existent, but only if we are talking about realities, not mere concepts.

This passage presents a rigorous logical argument against the view that ta'ayyun (determination) is non-existent ('adami). The opponent claims that if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be a negation of something, making it existent. The author responds by clarifying the terms: if we are dealing with concepts, the logic does not hold. Understanding this subtle distinction is crucial for grasping the nature of existence in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the structure: the opponent's claim followed by 'قلنا' (we said).
2
Identify the logical move
See how the argument says 'رفع العدمي ثبوتي' (removal of non-existent is existent). Understand that this is a standard kalam principle.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author concedes the principle but distinguishes between concepts and external realities. Notice the term 'مفهوم' (concept).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً للاتعين مطلقاً، أو لتعين آخر عدمي أو ثبوتي فيكون ثبوتياً لأن رفع العدمي ثبوتي، وحكم الأمثال واحد . قلنا : بعد المساعدة على أن العدمي عدم لشيء، وأن نقيضه ثبوتي إن أريد باللاتعين والتعين مفهوماً ،هما فلا حصرا وما صدقا عليه، فلا يلزم كون ما صدق عليه اللاتعين عدمياً.
Fourth: If it were non-existent, it would be a negation of the undetermined absolutely, or of another non-existent or existent determination, and thus it would be existent because the removal of the non-existent is existent, and the judgment of similar cases is one. We said: After conceding that the non-existent is a negation of something, and that its opposite is existent, if by 'undetermined' and 'determination' are meant concepts, then they do not refer to individuals and what they apply to; hence it does not follow that what the 'undetermined' applies to is non-existent.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the view that ta'ayyun is non-existent. The argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be a negation of either an absolute undetermined state or of another determination (whether non-existent or existent). In either case, it would be existent because removing a non-existent is an existent act. The author responds ('قلنا') by first conceding that a non-existent is a negation of something and that its opposite is existent. However, if by 'undetermined' and 'determination' we mean concepts in the mind, then they do not have specific referents, and what they apply to is not necessarily non-existent. The refutation hinges on the distinction between conceptual and external existence.

Key Terms
عدمي ('adami)
ثبوتي (wujudi)
رفع العدمي (removal of the non-existent)
مفهوم (concept)
لاتعين (undetermined)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that removing a non-existent always produces an existent entity in every context.
Correction: The author clarifies that this only applies to external realities, not when dealing with mere conceptual analysis.

Checkpoint
What is the key point in the author's refutation of the fourth argument?
A) That the non-existent cannot be removed
B) That 'undetermined' and 'determination' may be concepts, not external realities
C) That the argument is self-contradictory
D) That the opponent has conceded the point

Key Takeaway
The refutation hinges on distinguishing between concepts and external realities, showing that logical principles apply differently in each domain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a logical argument as true without checking whether the terms refer to concepts or reality? What in your life might be built on such an unchecked assumption?
?
When you read the opponent's argument, did you find it persuasive at first? What does your initial reaction reveal about your trust in seemingly flawless reasoning?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that are merely conceptual, yet you treat them as if they are real? How might your faith or actions change if you rigorously distinguished between the two?

The Fifth Argument Against Non-Existent Determination and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the fifth argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that if ta'ayyun were non-existent it would be common to individuals, and how the author refutes it by distinguishing absolute and particular determination.

Core Idea
The refutation hinges on distinguishing absolute determination from particular determination.

This passage presents the fifth argument against the view that ta'ayyun (determination) is non-existent. The argument claims that if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be common to all individuals and thus fail to individuate. The author responds by distinguishing two meanings of determination, showing that the argument only works if one meaning is conflated.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the argument (فإن كان...), then the response (قلنا...).
The argument uses conditional reasoning; follow the if-then logic.
2
Understand the argument's logic
The argument assumes that if ta'ayyun is non-existent, it must be a negation of something. It then considers two possibilities: either it negates absoluteness (or its equal), or it does not. In the first case, it would be common; in the second, it could be separated from the negation of absoluteness, leading to contradictions.
The two possibilities are exhaustive.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author disambiguates 'determination' into absolute determination (مطلق التعين) and particular determination (التعين الخاص). For absolute determination, commonality is not a problem because individuals are distinguished by particular determinations. For particular determination, the scenario of a thing being neither absolute nor determined is possible if it is determined by another determination.
This dissolves the argument by showing it conflates two meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً لما ينافيه، فإن كان عدماً للإطلاق، أو لما يساويه كان مشتركاً بين الأفراد كعدم الإطلاق، فلا يكون متميزاً، إن لم يكن لزم جواز انفكاكه عن عدم الإطلاق، إما بتحقق عدم الإطلاق بدونه فيكون الشيء لا مطلقاً، ولا معيناً وإما بالعكس فيكون مطلقاً ومعيناً. قلنا: إن أريد مطلق التعين لم يمتنع اشتراكه بين الأفراد، وتمايزها بالتعينات الخاصة وإن أريد التعين الخاص لم يمتنع كون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً لجواز أن يكون معيناً بتعين آخر).
Fifth: If it were non-existent, it would be a negation of what is incompatible with it. If it is a negation of absoluteness, or of what is equal to it, then it would be common among individuals like the negation of absoluteness, so it would not be distinct. If not, then it would be possible for it to be separable from the negation of absoluteness, either by the actualization of the negation of absoluteness without it, so the thing would be neither absolute nor determined, or the opposite, so it would be absolute and determined. We said: If absolute determination is meant, its being common among individuals is not impossible, and their differentiation is by particular determinations. If particular determination is meant, then it is not impossible for a thing to be neither absolute nor determined, because it is possible for it to be determined by another determination.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would be a negation of something incompatible with it. If it negates 'absoluteness' (i.e., being unrestricted), it would be common to all individuals (like the negation of absoluteness is common), so it could not distinguish individuals. If not, then it could be separated from the negation of absoluteness, leading to contradictions like a thing being neither absolute nor determined, or being both. The author responds by distinguishing two meanings: (1) if 'absolute determination' is meant, it is not impossible for it to be common among individuals since they differ by particular determinations; (2) if 'particular determination' is meant, then a thing can be neither absolute nor determined because it might be determined by another determination (i.e., a different particular). Thus the argument fails to prove impossibility.

Key Terms
تعين مطلق
تعين خاص
عدم الإطلاق
انفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if ta'ayyun is common, it cannot individuate.
Correction: The author shows that absolute determination can be common while particular determinations individuate, so commonality does not undermine the role of ta'ayyun.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response to the fifth argument, what is the key distinction that undermines the argument?
A) The distinction between existence and non-existence
B) The distinction between absolute and particular determination
C) The distinction between concept and reality
D) The distinction between essence and accident

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument fails because it conflates absolute determination (which can be common) with particular determination (which individuates).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever settle for an 'absolute determination' of your identity as a Muslim without working out the 'particular determination' of how that plays out in your daily actions and choices? What concrete step can you take today to make your faith more particular?
?
The argument warns against conflating two meanings of the same term. In your understanding of God's attributes (like His will or power), is there a danger of conflating absolute and particular meanings? How can this logic help safeguard your aqeedah from confusion?
