The First Proof That Ta'ayyun Is Existential and Its Refutation
The author presents the first proof that ta'ayyun is existential, refutes it, addresses al-Mawaqif's objection, and gives a counter-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument that ta'ayyun is a part of the determined thing and how it is refuted, including an objection from al-Mawaqif and the author's reply.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun is not an existential part; it is an accident or a conceptual construct.

This passage presents the first proof for the claim that ta'ayyun is an existential attribute. The proof argues that since the determined thing is existent and ta'ayyun is part of its concept, ta'ayyun must also be existent. The author refutes this by clarifying that the composite of quiddity and determination is merely conceptual. An objection from al-Mawaqif challenges this refutation, and the author offers a nuanced response involving several concessions.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its refutation
Read the first proof that ta'ayyun is a part of the determined thing, then the author's immediate refutation distinguishing two meanings of 'determined thing'.
Notice how the refutation uses a distinction between accident and part.
2
Understand al-Mawaqif's objection
Read al-Mawaqif's objection: the determined thing is a particular individual, not a universal, so ta'ayyun is part of an existent.
He argues that 'Zayd' is not just 'man' but man plus ta'ayyun.
3
Analyze the author's response
Examine the author's three-part answer: (i) the concept may be man restricted by accidents, (ii) a part of the concept need not exist externally, (iii) if it does, it is the sensible accidents, not ta'ayyun itself.
The response shows that the proof fails to establish ta'ayyun as an existential part.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider the distinction between 'ta'ayyun' (determination) and 'ma bihi al-ta'ayyun' (that by which determination occurs). The author ends by noting that the sensible accidents are causes, not the determination itself.
This clarifies the terminological precision required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي القائل بكون التعين وجودياً بوجوه : الأول : أنه جزء المتعين لكونه عبارة عن الماهية مع التعين وهو موجود وجزء الموجود موجود بالضرورة. وأجيبه : بأنه إن أريد بالمتعين الموصوف بالتعين، فظاهر أن التعين عارض له لا جزء منه ، وإن أريد المجموع المركب منهما، فلا نسلم أنه موجود، فإن الوصف إذا كان من الأعراض المحسوسة كما في الجسم الأبيض لم يكن المجموع إلا مركباً اعتبارياً، فكيف إذا كان مما وجوده نفس المتنازع، واعترض صاحب المواقف : بأن المراد بالمتعين هو ذلك الشخص المعلوم وجوده بالضرورة كزيد مثلاً ، وليس مفهومه مجرد مفهوم الانسان وإلا لصدق على عمرو، بل الإنسان مع شيء آخر يسميه التعين فيكون جزءاً من زيد الموجود فيكون موجوداً . والجواب : أنا سلمنا أن ليس مفهومه مفهوم الإنسان الكلي الصادق على عمرو. ولكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون هو الانسان المقيد بالعوارض المخصوصة المشخصة التي لا تصدق على غيره دون المجموع ولو سلم فجزء المفهوم لا يلزم أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج ولو سلم فذلك الشيء هو ما يخصه من الكم والكيف والأين ونحو ذلك ، مما يعلم وجوده بالضرورة من غير نزاع لكون أكثرها من المحسوسات، وهم لا يسمونها التعين بل ما به التعين
That is, the one who says that ta'ayyun is existential has proofs: First: It is a part of the determined thing because it is an expression of the quiddity with determination, and it is existent, and the part of an existent is necessarily existent. The answer to it: If by 'determined thing' is meant the thing described by determination, then it is clear that determination is an accident of it, not a part of it. If the composite of the two is meant, then we do not concede that it is existent, for if the attribute is among sensible accidents, like whiteness in a white body, the composite is only a conceptual composite. How much more so if it is something whose existence is the very object of dispute? And the author of al-Mawaqif objected: What is meant by 'the determined thing' is that particular individual known to exist necessarily, like Zayd for example. Its concept is not merely the concept of man, otherwise it would apply to Amr, but rather man together with something else called ta'ayyun, so it is a part of the existent Zayd and thus existent. The answer: We concede that its concept is not the universal concept of man applying to Amr. But why is it not permissible that it be man restricted by the particular distinguishing accidents that do not apply to others, without the composite? Even if conceded, a part of the concept is not necessarily existent in the external world. Even if conceded, that thing is what pertains to it of quantity, quality, location, and so forth, whose existence is known necessarily without dispute because most of them are sensible. And they do not call them ta'ayyun but rather that by which ta'ayyun occurs.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from those who claim ta'ayyun is existential (wujudi). The proof: ta'ayyun is part of the 'determined thing' (al-muta'ayyin) because the determined thing is the quiddity together with determination, and this composite is existent, so its part must be existent. The author ('وأجيبه') refutes this by noting two interpretations of 'determined thing': (1) if it means the quiddity described by determination, then ta'ayyun is an accident, not a part; (2) if it means the composite of quiddity and determination, then we do not concede that this composite is existent in reality, because if the attribute is a sensible accident (like whiteness in a white body), the composite is only conceptual; how much more so when the attribute's existence is itself the point of dispute? Then the author references an objection from 'sahib al-Mawaqif' (al-Iji or al-Taftazani?) who argues that the determined thing is a particular individual like Zayd, whose concept is not just 'man' but man plus something else (ta'ayyun), thus ta'ayyun is part of an existent and therefore existent. The author's answer ('والجواب') consists of several concessions and counterpoints: (i) we concede the concept is not the universal man, but why can it not be man restricted by particular accidents? (ii) even if ta'ayyun is part of the concept, it does not entail external existence; (iii) even if it does, that something is actually the set of particularizing accidents (quantity, quality, location, etc.), which are sensible and known to exist, but they are not called ta'ayyun but rather 'that by which ta'ayyun occurs' (ma bihi al-ta'ayyun). Thus the proof fails to establish that ta'ayyun itself is an existent part.

Key Terms
متعين (determined thing)
ماهية (quiddity)
عارض (accident)
مركب اعتباري (conceptual composite)
صاحب المواقف (author of al-Mawaqif)
ما به التعين (that by which determination occurs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since the determined thing is a single existent entity, all its conceptual parts must be existent in the same way.
Correction: The author shows that the composite of quiddity and determination is only conceptual, not externally existent; a part of a concept does not entail external existence.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that ta'ayyun is a part of the determined thing because the determined thing is existent?
A) He agrees but adds conditions
B) He argues that the composite is only conceptual, not externally existent
C) He denies that the determined thing is existent
D) He says ta'ayyun is not a part but the whole

Key Takeaway
The first proof that ta'ayyun is existential fails because the determined thing is either an accident or a merely conceptual composite, and even if ta'ayyun is part of the concept, that does not guarantee external existence; the real particularizing accidents are what exist, not ta'ayyun itself.

?
In this lesson, ta'ayyun is not an existential part but a relational concept. When you think about Allah's attributes, do you ever unconsciously treat them as separate parts added to His essence? How can this lesson's precision guard you against such a subtle shirk in your understanding?

The Second and Third Proofs for Ta'ayyun as Existential
Two proofs for ta'ayyun as existential and the start of the refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third proofs for the existential nature of ta'ayyun and the beginning of the response that challenges them.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun must be existential because it multiplies nature and gives identity.

The author continues his presentation of arguments for ta'ayyun being a positive existent. He offers two more proofs: one based on the multiplication of a specific nature by added accidents, and another based on the impossibility of a non-existent determining anything. He then begins a joint refutation of both arguments. This passage shows the dialectical structure of kalam: every proof invites a counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note how each proof argues from a different angle: the second from multiplication of nature, the third from identity.
The proofs are introduced with 'الثاني' and 'الثالث'.
2
Observe the joint refutation
The author begins the answer with 'والجواب عنهما' (and the answer to both) but the text cuts off. Notice the incomplete sentence and consider what the author might say next.
The refutation is incomplete; the full argument continues elsewhere.
3
Reflect on the dialectical structure
Think about why the response addresses both proofs together. What common assumption might they share? This is a key skill in kalam: identifying shared premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الطبيعة النوعية كالإنسان مثلاً لا تتكثر بنفسها، لما سبق من أن الماهية من حيث هي لا تقتضي الوحدة والكثرة، وإنما تتكثر بما ينضاف إليها من العوارض الموجودة المخصوصة التي ربما تكون محسوسة وهو المراد بالتشخص . الثالث : أن التعين لو كان عدمياً لما كان متعيناً في نفسه، إذ لا هوية للمعدوم، فلم يكن معيناً لغيره ضرورة أن ما لا ثبوت له، لا يصلح سبباً لتميز الشيء عما عداه بحسب الخارج والجواب عنهما أن ما ينضاف إلى الطبيعة،
Second: That the specific nature, like man for example, does not multiply by itself, because it has preceded that quiddity qua quiddity does not necessitate unity or multiplicity; rather it is multiplied by what is added to it of particular existent accidents, which perhaps are sensible, and that is what is meant by tashakhkhus (individualization). Third: That if determination were non-existent, it would not be determined in itself, because the non-existent has no identity, so it would not determine another, necessarily because what has no existence cannot be a cause for distinguishing a thing from other things in external reality. And the answer to both: That what is added to the nature...
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents two additional proofs for the existential status of ta'ayyun. The second proof: the specific nature (e.g., humanity) does not multiply by itself; quiddity as such does not require unity or multiplicity, but rather is multiplied by added particular existent accidents (which may be sensible), and this addition is tashakhkhus (individualization). So ta'ayyun must be existent since it is that which multiplies the nature. The third proof: if ta'ayyun were non-existent, it would not be determined in itself, because the non-existent has no identity, so it could not determine another thing; something with no existence cannot be a cause for distinguishing one thing from another in the external world. The author begins his response ('والجواب عنهما') addressing both proofs together: 'That what is added to the nature...' but the text breaks off. The incomplete answer suggests the author will explain that what is added to the nature may not be a distinct existent in the sense claimed. The passage is clearly part of a larger argument.

Objection from al-Mawaqif on Determination and Its Answer
Objection that determination is part of the determined thing, with multi-step answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that determination must be a part of the determined thing and the three-layered response, including the distinction between determination and individuation.

Core Idea
Determination is not a separate part; individuation is the addition of particular accidents.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection and answer from the kalam tradition. Al-Mawaqif's author argues that if 'determined' means more than the universal essence, then that extra element must be a real part, hence existent. The answer carefully shows that this extra element can be understood as accidents that do not form a composite, and even if they did, conceptual parts need not be external. Ultimately, the response clarifies that the real issue is about individuation (تشخص) through sensible accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Focus on the claim that the concept of 'determined' (متعين) is not just the universal human, so it must include determination as a part, making it existent.
Notice the parallel to Zaid and Amr.
2
Identify the answer's first concession
The answer concedes that the concept is not the universal, but suggests it could be the universal restricted by particularizing accidents, not a composite.
This avoids the need for an added part.
3
Follow the second concession
Even if it were a composite, a part of the concept does not have to exist externally.
مفهوم and وجود are distinguished.
4
Examine the third concession
Even if those parts exist externally, they are sensible accidents (quantity, quality, location) known necessarily, and they are not called 'determination' but 'individuation' (تشخص).
This clarifies terminology.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Connect the answer to the broader principle: quiddity does not entail multiplicity; it becomes multiple by accidents.
Review the key terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض صاحب المواقف : بأن المراد بالمتعين هو ذلك الشخص المعلوم وجوده بالضرورة كزيد مثلاً ، وليس مفهومه مجرد مفهوم الانسان وإلا لصدق على عمرو، بل الإنسان مع شيء آخر يسميه التعين فيكون جزءاً من زيد الموجود فيكون موجوداً . والجواب : أنا سلمنا أن ليس مفهومه مفهوم الانسان الكلي الصادق على عمرو. ولكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون هو الانسان المقيد بالعوارض المخصوصة المشخصة التي لا تصدق على غيره دون المجموع ولو سلم فجزء المفهوم لا يلزم أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج ولو سلم فذلك الشيء هو ما يخصه من الكم والكيف والأين ونحو ذلك ، مما يعلم وجوده بالضرورة من غير نزاع لكون أكثرها من المحسوسات، وهم لا يسمونها التعين بل ما به التعين الثاني، أن الطبيعة النوعية كالإنسان مثلاً لا تتكثر بنفسها، لما سبق من أن الماهية من حيث هي لا تقتضي الوحدة والكثرة، وإنما تتكثر بما ينضاف إليهما من العوارض الموجودة المخصوصة التي ربما تكون محسوسة وهو المراد بالتشخص .
Al-Mawaqif's author objected: that what is meant by 'determined' is that specific person known necessarily to exist, like Zaid for example, and its concept is not merely the concept of 'human', otherwise it would be true of 'Amr. Rather, [it is] 'human' along with something else that he calls 'determination', so it would be a part of Zaid as existent, hence existent. The answer: We concede that its concept is not the universal concept of 'human' which is true of 'Amr. But why may it not be 'human' restricted by the specific particularizing accidental properties which are not true of another, without the composite? And even if conceded, a part of the concept does not necessarily have to exist externally. And even if conceded, that thing is what particularizes it from quantity, quality, location, and the like, which are known necessarily to exist without dispute, since most of them are sensible. And they do not call them 'determination', but rather 'that by which is the secondary determination', that the specific nature like 'human' for example does not become multiple by itself, because of what preceded that quiddity in itself does not entail unity or multiplicity, but rather becomes multiple by the specific existent accidental properties that are added to them, which may be sensible, and that is what is meant by individuation.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the author of al-Mawaqif and its answer. The objection argues that the concept of 'determined' refers to a particular person known necessarily to exist, like Zaid. This concept is not simply the universal 'human', otherwise it would apply to 'Amr as well. Rather, it consists of 'human' plus something else called 'determination', which would then be a part of the existing Zaid and must exist. The answer concedes that the concept is not the universal 'human'. However, it argues that it could be 'human' qualified by particular accidents that do not apply to others, without needing a composite. Even if the concept has parts, a conceptual part does not necessarily exist externally. Furthermore, those particularizing features are matters of quantity, quality, location, etc., known necessarily and sensible. The answer notes that these are not called 'determination' but 'that by which secondary determination occurs'. The response concludes that specific natures do not become multiple by themselves, but only by the addition of these existent specific accidents, which is what individuation means.

Key Terms
تعين
عوارض مشخصة
تشخص
طبيعة نوعية
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That determination is a separate entity added to the essence, like a part of a composite.
Correction: The answer shows that determination can be understood as the essence qualified by particular accidents, not a separate part. Even if viewed as a part, conceptual parts need not be external. Ultimately, what is added are sensible accidents, which are not determination but individuation.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does a part of a concept not necessarily exist externally?
A) Because concepts are purely mental.
B) Because the part is the universal which is abstract.
C) Because conceptual parts have no external reference.
D) Because the concept of determination is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Determination is not an added part; individuation through accidents explains particularity.

Third Argument on Determination and Its Answer
Third argument and its answer regarding determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the view that determination is non-existent and how the answer resolves it by identifying particularizing accidents as existent determinates.

Core Idea
A non-existent cannot cause determination; the real cause is particularizing accidents.

This lesson examines a key argument in the debate over the nature of determination (تعين). The argument claims that if determination were non-existent (عدمياً), it could not be determinate itself nor serve to distinguish things. The answer shifts the focus to particularizing accidents (عوارض مشخصة) which are existent, thereby refuting the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument and answer as a unit.
Note the logical flow: premise → conclusion → answer.
2
Analyze the argument structure
Identify the logical steps: if determination is non-existent → no identity → cannot be determinate itself → cannot serve as a cause of distinction.
3
Understand the answer
The answer states that what is added to the nature and determines it are particularizing accidents, whose existence is conceded.
This counters the claim that determination is non-existent.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this answer preserves determination as something existent, consistent with earlier discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن التعين لو كان عدمياً لما كان متعيناً في نفسه، إذ لا هوية للمعدوم، فلم يكن معيناً لغيره ضرورة أن ما لا ثبوت له، لا يصلح سبباً لتميز الشيء عما عداه بحسب الخارج والجواب عنهما أن ما ينضاف إلى الطبيعة، ويعينها ويكثرها هي العوارض المشخصة، ولا نزاع في وجودها على ما سبق .
Third: That if determination were non-existent, it would not be determinate in itself, since there is no identity for a non-existent. So it would not be determinate for other, necessarily, since that which has no existence cannot be a cause for distinguishing a thing from others according to external [existence]. The answer to both [arguments?]: that which is added to the nature and determines and multiplies it are the particularizing accidents, and there is no dispute about their existence, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the view that determination is non-existent. The argument runs: if determination were non-existent, it would have no identity and therefore could not be determinate itself, nor could it serve to distinguish one thing from another. The answer responds that what is added to the specific nature and makes it determinate and multiple are the particularizing accidents, whose existence is conceded. Thus determination is not a non-existent but rather these accidents, which are existent.

Correction: The argument shows that a non-existent lacks identity and cannot be a cause; the answer provides that particularizing accidents, which are existent, are the real determinates.

The Fourth Argument for Determination and Its Refutation
Fourth argument via contradiction and its systematic response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument for determination's existence based on the law of excluded middle and how the response deconstructs its assumptions about non-existence and contradiction.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not always a privation; its contradictory is not always existent.

The fourth argument uses a logical dilemma to argue that determination must be existent. It assumes that a non-existent must be a privation of some specific thing, and that the contradictory of a non-existent is existent. The response challenges each of these assumptions, distinguishing between mere absence and privation. Let's follow the reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument's dilemma
Read the argument carefully. It states: if determination were non-existent (but not absolute non-existence), it must be either a non-existence of non-determination or a non-existence of determination itself. Both options are claimed to force determination to be existent.
The argument relies on the law of excluded middle and the rule that the contradictory of a non-existent is existent.
2
Identify the response's denials
The response makes several denials: (1) a non-existent need not be a non-existence of something; (2) the contradictory of a non-existent is not always existent (example: blindness/not-blindness); (3) if concepts are meant, there is no restriction; (4) if external application is meant, not everything with non-determination is non-existent; (5) determinations may not be similar.
Each denial targets a specific premise of the argument.
3
Reflect on the blindness example
The example 'blindness and not-blindness' shows that the negation of a privation (blindness) does not yield a positive existence (not-blindness is just the absence of blindness). This undercuts the claim that the contradictory of a non-existent is always existent.
This example is crucial for understanding the response's logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: : أن التعين لو كان عدمياً وليس عدماً مطلقاً لكان عدماً للاتعين مطلقاً، أو لتعين إذ لا مخرج عن النقيضين، وذلك التعين إما عدمي أو ثبوتي، وعلى التقارير يلزم كونه وجودياً. إما على الأولين، فلأن نقيض العدمي وجودي، وإما على الثالث فلأن حكم الأمثال واحد . والجواب : أنا لا نسلم أن العدمي يلزم أن يكون عدماً لأمر ما ، بل يكون معدوماً في الخارج على ما ادعينا من أنه اعتباري. ولو سلم، فلا نسلم أن نقيض العدمي وجودي كالعمى واللاعمى، ولو سلم فإن أريد بالتعين، واللاتعين مفهومهما، فلا حصر لجواز أن يكون التعين عدماً لمفهوم آخر. وإن أريد ما صدق عليه فلا نسلم أن كل ما يصدق عليه اللاتعين فهو عدمي، ليكون نقيضه ثبوتياً . كيف واللاتعين صادق على جميع الحقائق، ولو سلم فلا نسلم تماثل التعينات، لم لا يجوز أن تكون متخالفة متشاركة في عارض هو مفهوم التعين .
Fourth: That if determination were non-existent and not absolute non-existence, it would be a non-existence of non-determination absolutely, or a non-existence of determination [itself], since there is no exit from the two contradictories. And that determination is either non-existent or existent. In all reports, it would entail that it is existent. Either on the first two [suppositions?], because the contradictory of a non-existent is existent, or on the third, because the rule of similars is one. The answer: We do not concede that a non-existent must be a non-existence of something. Rather, it is non-existent externally according to what we claimed that it is considered. Even if conceded, we do not concede that the contradictory of a non-existent is existent, like blindness and not-blindness. Even if conceded, if what is meant by determination and non-determination is their concepts, then there is no restriction; it is possible that determination is a non-existence of another concept. If what is meant is what is applied [to them externally], then we do not concede that everything that non-determination is applied to is non-existent, such that its contradictory would be existent. How so? For non-determination applies to all realities. Even if conceded, we do not concede the similarity of determinations; why may they not be different, sharing in an accident which is the concept of determination?
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument for the existence of determination. The argument uses the law of excluded middle: if determination were non-existent (but not absolute non-existence), it would have to be either a non-existence of non-determination or a non-existence of determination itself, leading to contradictions that force determination to be existent. The answer systematically denies premises. First, it denies that a non-existent must be a non-existence of something specific; it can be simply lacking external existence and be a mental consideration. Second, even if conceded, the contradictory of a non-existent is not always existent, as shown by the example of blindness and not-blindness. Third, if considering concepts, there is no restriction; determination could be a non-existence of some other concept. Fourth, if considering external application, not everything to which non-determination applies is non-existent, since non-determination applies to all realities. Finally, even if conceded, determinations may differ and only share in the accident of the concept of determination.

Key Terms
تعين
لاتعين
عدم
وجود
نقيض
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every non-existent must be a non-existence of something specific.
Correction: Non-existence can simply be a mental consideration without external reality; it need not be a privation of a specific thing.

Checkpoint
What example is given to show that the contradictory of a non-existent is not always existent?
A) Black and white.
B) Blindness and not-blindness.
C) Existence and non-existence.
D) Determination and non-determination.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because it wrongly assumes that non-existence is always a privation of a specific thing and that the contradictory of a non-existent is always existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that an absence must always be a lack of something specific, rather than simply not being there? How might this assumption affect your understanding of God's creation and His attributes?
?
The argument in this passage tries to force a conclusion by assuming that every non-existent must have an existent contradictory. Where in your own thinking do you implicitly accept similar dichotomies without questioning them?
?
When you encounter a logical proof that seems airtight, do you examine each premise as carefully as the responder did here? What is one belief you hold that you have never subjected to such rigorous scrutiny?

The Fourth Proof on Determination and Its Refutation
The author presents the fourth proof that determination is existential and refutes the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that determination (al-ta'ayyun) is non-existential by analyzing its logical structure and rebutting each premise.

Core Idea
A non-existential determination leads to contradictions, but the opponent can deny key premises.

This passage presents the fourth proof that determination must be existential, followed by its refutation. The opponent's proof uses a dilemma to force the conclusion that determination is existential. The author systematically dismantles this argument by denying that non-existential entails non-existence of something, challenging the nature of contradictories, and pointing out diversity among determinations. Understanding this dialectical exchange is crucial for grasping how theologians defend their positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the opponent's proof that determination, if non-existential but not absolute non-existence, leads to a dilemma forcing it to be existential. Notice the three cases considered.
The proof assumes that everything is either a contradictory pair.
2
Identify the refutation's structure
The author gives several 'if conceded' responses. Trace how each concession is either denied or shown not to lead to the desired conclusion.
The refutation has four main moves.
3
Analyze the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'determination', 'non-determination', 'existence', and 'non-existence'. Understand how their meanings affect the argument.
The author distinguishes between concept and instantiation.
4
Consider the broader significance
Reflect on why this debate matters—it concerns the nature of divine attributes and whether they are real or merely relational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أن التعين لو كان عدمياً وليس عدماً مطلقاً لكان عدماً للاتعين مطلقاً، أو لتعين إذ لا مخرج عن النقيضين، وذلك التعين إما عدمي أو ثبوتي، وعلى التقارير يلزم كونه وجودياً. إما على الأولين، فلأن نقيض العدمي وجودي، وإما على الثالث فلأن حكم الأمثال واحد. والجواب: أنا لا نسلم أن العدمي يلزم أن يكون عدماً لأمر ما، بل يكون معدوماً في الخارج على ما ادعينا من أنه اعتباري. ولو سلم، فلا نسلم أن نقيض العدمي وجودي كالعمى واللاعمى، ولو سلم فإن أريد بالتعين واللاتعين مفهومهما، فلا حصر لجواز أن يكون التعين عدماً لمفهوم آخر. وإن أريد ما صدق عليه فلا نسلم أن كل ما يصدق عليه اللاتعين فهو عدمي، ليكون نقيضه ثبوتياً. كيف واللاتعين صادق على جميع الحقائق، ولو سلم فلا نسلم تماثل التعينات، لم لا يجوز أن تكون متخالفة متشاركة في عارض هو مفهوم التعين.
The fourth: That determination, if it were non-existential and not an absolute non-existence, would be non-existence of the absolute non-determined, or of a determination, since there is no exit from the two contradictories, and that determination is either non-existential or existential, and on all accounts it follows that it is existential. Either in the first two cases, because the contradictory of the non-existential is existential, or in the third because the rule of similars is one. The answer: We do not concede that the non-existential must be a non-existence of something, rather it is non-existent in the external, as we claimed that it is relational. And if conceded, we do not concede that the contradictory of the non-existential is existential, like blindness and non-blindness. And if conceded, then if by determination and non-determination their concepts are meant, then there is no restriction, as it is possible that determination is non-existence of another concept. And if what is applied to is meant, then we do not concede that everything to which non-determination applies is non-existential, so that its contradictory would be existential. How? For non-determination applies to all realities. And if conceded, we do not concede the similarity of determinations; why is it not permissible that they be diverse, sharing an accident which is the concept of determination?
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that determination (al-ta'ayyun) must be existential (wujudi) rather than non-existential ('adami), followed by the author's refutation. The proof argues: if determination were non-existential but not absolute non-existence, it would be non-existence of either absolute non-determination or of a determination, with no third option. In both cases, the contradictory must be existential, so determination is existential. The author answers by denying that the non-existential must be non-existence of something; it can be merely relational. Even if conceded, the contradictory of the non-existential is not always existential (e.g., blindness vs. non-blindness are both non-existential). Furthermore, if 'determination' refers to a concept, its opposite could be non-existence of another concept. If it refers to instantiations, not everything non-determinate is non-existential. Finally, determinations may be diverse, not similar, sharing only the accidental concept of determination.

Key Terms
التعين
العدم
الوجود
اللاتعين
النقيضان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that to refute a claim it suffices to show an opponent's premise leads to contradiction, but the author shows the opponent can deny the premise.
Correction: Refutation often requires addressing each premise individually; the opponent may deny one of the premises in the dilemma, as the author does.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what does the author's opponent argue about the nature of determination?
A) It is existential.
B) It is non-existential.
C) It is both existential and non-existential.
D) It is neither existential nor non-existential.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the proof by denying that non-existential entails non-existence of something, and by showing that the opponent's assumptions about contradictories, concepts, and similarity of determinations are not forced.

The Objection: Ta'ayyun as a Necessary Absence
The objector presents a dilemma to force ta'ayyun to be identical to the absence of absoluteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that forces ta'ayyun to be identified with the absence of absoluteness.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun must be the absence of absoluteness, otherwise contradictions arise.

The author first presents a powerful objection against the view that ta'ayyun (determination) is not simply the absence of absoluteness. The objector argues that if ta'ayyun is not that absence, it leads to either a thing being neither absolute nor determined (removing contradictories) or both at once (conjoining contradictories). This dilemma seems to force the conclusion that ta'ayyun is merely negative.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the two-pronged argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: if X then Y, else if not X then Z.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection considers two possibilities: either ta'ayyun is the absence of absoluteness or not. If not, then either absence of absoluteness exists without ta'ayyun (leading to neither absolute nor determined) or ta'ayyun exists without absence of absoluteness (leading to both absolute and determined). Both are contradictions.
3
Understand why the objector thinks ta'ayyun must be absence
The objector assumes that 'adam al-iṭlāq is common to all individuals, so if ta'ayyun were something else, it could not distinguish them. So ta'ayyun must be that absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدمه عن عدم الإطلاق، وعدم الإطلاق متحقق في جميع الأفراد، فكذا التعين فلا يكون متميزاً، فلا يكون تعيناً، وإن لم يكن التعين عدماً للإطلاق، ولا عدماً لما لا ينفك عدمه عن عدم الإطلاق، لزم جواز (١) الانفكاك بين عدم الإطلاق بدون التعين، فيلزم كون (٢) الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا متعيناً، وفيه رفع للنقيضين، وإما أن يتحقق التعين بدون عدم الإطلاق، فيلزم كون الشيء مطلقاً ومتعيناً وفيه جمع للنقيضين. والجواب: أنه إن أريد بالتعين الذي يجعله (٣) عدم الإطلاق مطلق التعين، فلا نسلم ثم (٤) امتناع اشتراكه بين الأفراد كعدم الإطلاق، وإنما يمتنع لو لم يكن تمايز الأفراد بالتعينات الخاصة (٥) المعروضة لمطلق التعين، وإن أريد بالتعين (٦) التعين الخاص فنختار أنه ليس عدماً للإطلاق ولا لما لا ينفك عدمه عن عدم (٧) الإطلاق، بل لأمر يوجد عدم الإطلاق بدون عدمه، الذي هو ذلك التعين، وهو لا يستلزم إلا كون الشيء لا مطلقاً ولا معيناً بذلك التعين، ولا استحالة في ذلك لجواز أن يكون معيناً بتعين آخر.
Its non-existence is due to the non-existence of absoluteness, and non-existence of absoluteness is realized in all individuals, so likewise al-ta'ayyun (determination) so it is not distinct, so it is not a determination. And if al-ta'ayyun is not a non-existence of absoluteness, nor a non-existence of that whose non-existence is inseparable from the non-existence of absoluteness, then the separation between non-existence of absoluteness without al-ta'ayyun would be possible, leading to the thing being neither absolute nor determined, and that is a removal of the two contradictories. Or if al-ta'ayyun is realized without non-existence of absoluteness, then the thing would be both absolute and determined, and that is a conjunction of the two contradictories. The answer: If what is meant by al-ta'ayyun that negates absoluteness is absolute determination, then we do not accept the impossibility of its sharing among individuals like the non-existence of absoluteness; it would only be impossible if individuals were not distinguished by specific determinations that qualify absolute determination. And if what is meant by al-ta'ayyun is specific determination, then we choose that it is not a non-existence of absoluteness nor a non-existence of that whose non-existence is inseparable from the non-existence of absoluteness, but rather it is something for which the non-existence of absoluteness exists without its non-existence, which is that determination, and it entails nothing except that the thing is neither absolute nor determined by that determination, and there is no impossibility in that because it may be determined by another determination.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding whether ta'ayyun (determination) is negative or positive. The objector attempts to force a dilemma: if ta'ayyun is not the absence of absoluteness ('adam al-iṭlāq) nor inseparable from it, then either ta'ayyun must exist without absence of absoluteness (leading to both absolute and determined, a contradiction) or absence of absoluteness exists without ta'ayyun (leading to neither absolute nor determined, also a contradiction). Therefore, ta'ayyun must be identical to the absence of absoluteness. The answer distinguishes two senses of ta'ayyun: absolute determination and specific determination. If by ta'ayyun is meant absolute determination, then it is not impossible for it to be shared among individuals, just as the absence of absoluteness is shared. The impossibility of sharing applies only to specific determinations that differentiate individuals. If by ta'ayyun is meant specific determination, then it is neither the absence of absoluteness nor something inseparable from it. Rather, it is something such that the absence of absoluteness can exist without it. This does not lead to a contradiction because the thing may be determined by another determination. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun)
عدم الإطلاق ('adam al-iṭlāq)
رفع النقيضين (raf' al-naqīḍayn)
جمع النقيضين (jam' al-naqīḍayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what happens if ta'ayyun is not the absence of absoluteness and is not inseparable from it?
A) The thing becomes absolute.
B) The thing becomes neither absolute nor determined.
C) The thing becomes both absolute and determined.
D) The thing remains unknown.

Key Takeaway
The objection forces a dilemma that seems to require ta'ayyun to be nothing more than the absence of absoluteness.

?

The Answer: Distinguishing Absolute and Specific Determination
The author answers by distinguishing two meanings of ta'ayyun and showing the objection fails in both cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the dilemma by distinguishing between absolute determination and specific determination.

Core Idea
The dilemma dissolves once we distinguish between absolute and specific determination.

The author now answers the objection by making a crucial distinction. He argues that the objection confuses two different meanings of ta'ayyun. If by ta'ayyun one means 'absolute determination' (muṭlaq al-ta'ayyun), then it can be shared among individuals, just like the absence of absoluteness. If one means 'specific determination' (al-ta'ayyun al-khāṣṣ), then it is neither the absence of absoluteness nor something inseparable from it; rather, it is a positive attribute that does not lead to contradiction because the thing may still be determined by another determination.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see how the author disarms the objection.
2
Identify the two senses of ta'ayyun
The author distinguishes between absolute determination and specific determination. Trace how each sense avoids the dilemma.
Note the phrasing: 'مطلق التعين' and 'التعين الخاص'.
3
Understand why specific determination is not a contradiction
The author states that specific determination is not the absence of absoluteness, but it does not lead to contradiction because it is possible for the thing to be determined by another determination.

Source Text

Basic Definitions of Wujudi, 'Adami, I'tibari, and Haqiqi
The author defines the four attribute types: existential, non-existential, real, and mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic definitions of the four attribute types (existential, non-existential, real, and mental) and why they are essential for discussing individuation.

Core Idea
Attributes are classified by their mode of existence: real, mental, existential, or non-existential.

In this passage, the author provides concise definitions of the four key terms used to describe attributes in kalam. These terms form the foundation for determining whether individuation is existential, non-existential, or mental. Let's examine them closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how each term is defined by its opposite: wujudi vs 'adami, haqiqi vs i'tibari.
The author gives multiple meanings for 'adami.
2
Identify the two pairs
Pair wujudi with 'adami, and haqiqi with i'tibari. Understand that 'adami can refer to the non-existent, to the absence of a thing, or to a concept that includes non-existence.
3
Connect to individuation
The passage ends by stating that after clarifying these terms, the truth about individuation becomes clear. Reflect on why these definitions are needed.
Individuation (tashakhkhus) is introduced as the accident, thisness, or non-acceptance of sharing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنتهي إلى ما يفيد الهذية، والعدمي قد يطلق على المعدوم، وعلى عدم أمر ما، وعلى ما يدخل في مفهومه العدم، والوجودي بخلافه، والحقيقي على ما هو ثابت في نفس الأمر من غير شائبة الفرض والتقدير والاعتباري بخلافه. فبعد تلخيص المراد بالثبوتي والعدمي، وأن التشخص هو تلك العوارض، أو ما يحصل عندها من الهذية، أو كون الفرد بحيث لا يقبل الشركة، أو عدم قبوله لذلك كان الحق جلياً.

تصور الشيء بوجه ما وإن كان كافياً في الحكم عليه في الجملة. لكن خصوصيات الأحكام ربما تستدعي تصورات مخصوصة، لا بد منها في صحة الحكم، فلا بد في تحقيق أن التعين وجودي أو عدمي اعتباري أو غير اعتباري من بيان ما هو المراد من هذه الألفاظ.

فنقول: الحقيقة النوعية المتحصلة بنفسها، أو بما لها من الذاتيات قد يلحقها كثرة بحسب ما يعرض لها من الكميات والكيفيات، والأوضاع والإضافات واختلاف المواد وغير ذلك، وربما تنتهي العوارض إلى ما يفيد الهذية، وامتناع الشركة كهذا الانسان وذاك، وتسمى العوارض المشخصة، فلا بد في تحصيل موضوع القضية المطلوبة من بيان أن المراد بالتشخص هو العوارض، أو ما يحصل عندها من الهذية، أو عدم قبول الشركة، أو كون الحصة من النوع بهذه الحيثية، أو نحو ذلك، ثم لا بد لتحصيل معنى المحمول من بيان المراد بالوجودي، والعدمي، والاعتباري

فقيل: العدمي المعدوم، وقيل ما يكون عدماً مطلقاً، أو مضافاً متركباً مع وجودي كعدم البصر عما من شأنه، أو غير متركب كعدم قبول الشركة، وقيل، ما يدخل في مفهومه العدم ككون الشيء بحيث لا يقبل الشركة. والوجودي بخلافه، فهو الموجود، أو الوجود مطلقاً، أو مضافاً، أو ما لا يدخل في مفهومه العدم، والعبرة بالمعنى دون اللفظ، حتى إن العمى عدمي، واللاعدم وجودي، وفي المواقف أن الوجودي ما يكون ثبوته لموصوفه بوجوده له، أي بحسب الخارج نحو السواد لا أن يكون ذلك باعتبار وجودهما في العقل واتصاف موصوفه به فيه أي في العقل دون الخارج كالإمكان، وهو أعم من الموجود، لجواز وجودي لا يعرض له الوجود أبداً، لكنه بحيث إذا ثبت للموصوف، كان ذلك بوجوده له. وهذا معنى ما قال القاضي الأرموي، إذا قلنا لشيء إنه وجودي، لا نعني أنه دائم الوجود، بل نعني أنه مفهوم يصح أن يعرض له الوجود الخارجي عند قيامه بموجود، وعند قيامه بمعدوم لا يكون له وجود وكأنه يريد الأعم من وجه، وإلا فمن الموجود ما لا يسمى وجودياً كالانسان، وغيره من المفهومات المستقلة، وأما الاعتباري فهو ما لا تحقق له إلا بحسب فرض العقل، وإن كان موصوفه متصفاً به في نفس الأمر كالإمكان، فإن الإنسان متصف به في نفس الأمر، بمعنى أنه بحيث إذا نسبه العقل إلى الوجود يعقل له وصفاً هو الإمكان، ويقابله الحقيقي إذا تقرر هذا فلا خفاء في أن العوارض المشخصة وجودية، والهذية اعتبارية، وتميز الفرد عما عداه وعدم قبوله الشركة وكونه ليس غيره أو لا يقبل الشركة عدمية.
Ending with what gives ha-dhiyya (thisness). And al-'adami (the non-existential) may be applied to the non-existent, to the absence of a certain thing, and to that in whose concept non-existence enters. And al-wujudi (the existential) is opposite to it. And al-haqiqi (the real) is that which is established in the matter itself without any admixture of supposition and estimation, and al-i'tibari (the mental) is opposite to it. After summarizing the intended meaning of al-thubuti (positive) and al-'adami, and that al-tashakhkhus (individuation) is those accidents, or what occurs from them of ha-dhiyya, or the individual being such that it does not admit of sharing, or its non-acceptance of that, the truth was clear.

Conceiving a thing in some respect, even if sufficient for judging it in general, however the particularities of judgments may require particular conceptions, necessary for the correctness of the judgment. Therefore, in verifying whether determination is existential or non-existential, mental or non-mental, it is necessary to clarify what is meant by these terms.

We say: The specific essence, subsisting by itself or by its essential attributes, may be accompanied by multiplicity according to what occurs to it of quantities, qualities, positions, relations, differences of matter, and other things. And the accidents may end with what gives ha-dhiyya and prevention of sharing, like this human and that. They are called al-'awarid al-mushakhkhisa (individuating accidents). So to obtain the subject of the desired proposition, it is necessary to clarify whether by al-tashakhkhus is meant the accidents, or what occurs from them of ha-dhiyya, or non-acceptance of sharing, or the portion of the species being in this respect, or something like that. Then to obtain the meaning of the predicate, it is necessary to clarify what is meant by al-wujudi, al-'adami, and al-i'tibari.

So it was said: al-'adami is the non-existent. And it was said: it is absolute non-existence, or relative compounded with an existential, like lack of sight from what should have it, or non-compounded like non-acceptance of sharing. And it was said: it is what enters its concept non-existence, like a thing being such that it does not accept sharing. And al-wujudi is opposite to it, so it is the existent, or existence absolutely, or relative, or what does not enter its concept non-existence. And consideration is of meaning, not wording, so that blindness is 'adami, and non-blindness is wujudi. And in al-Mawaqif, al-wujudi is that whose affirmation for its subject is by its existence for it, i.e. externally like blackness, not that it is by consideration of their existence in the mind and the subject's being described by it in the mind, i.e. in the mind without the external like al-imkan (possibility). And it is more general than the existent, because it is possible that there is a wujudi that never has existence occur to it, but it is such that when it is established for the subject, that is by its existence for it. And this is the meaning of what al-Qadi al-Urmawi said: When we say of something that it is wujudi, we do not mean that it is eternally existent, but we mean that it is a concept which it is valid for external existence to occur to it when it subsists in an existent, and when it subsists in a non-existent, it has no existence. It is as if he intends the more general in one respect, otherwise among existents there is what is not called wujudi like man and other independent concepts. As for al-i'tibari, it is what has no reality except according to the supposition of the mind, even if its subject is described by it in the matter itself like imkan, for man is described by it in the matter itself, meaning that he is such that when the mind relates him to existence, it conceives for him an attribute which is imkan. And al-haqiqi is opposite to it. If this is established, then there is no hiddenness in that the individuating accidents are wujudi, and ha-dhiyya is i'tibari, and the distinction of the individual from other than it, and its non-acceptance of sharing, and its being not other, or its not accepting sharing, are 'adami.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and classification of key terms used in kalam to describe attributes and their role in individuation. The author begins by summarizing the meanings of thubuti (positive) and 'adami (non-existential), and then lists the various interpretations of tashakhkhus (individuation): it could be the accidents themselves, the 'thisness' (ha-dhiyya) arising from them, the individual's non-acceptance of sharing, or the absence of such acceptance. He then states that the truth is clear after this summary.

Next, he explains that while a general conception of a thing may suffice for judgment, particular judgments require precise concepts. Therefore, to determine whether determination (ta'ayyun) is existential, non-existential, mental, or real, we must clarify the terms.

He then defines al-haqiqa al-naw'iyya (specific essence) and explains that it may be accompanied by multiplicity due to various accidents (quantities, qualities, positions, relations, etc.). These accidents can culminate in ha-dhiyya and prevention of sharing, and are called al-'awarid al-mushakhkhisa (individuating accidents). To obtain the subject of a proposition, we must clarify tashakhkhus; to obtain the predicate, we must clarify al-wujudi, al-'adami, and al-i'tibari.

On page 447, he presents multiple definitions of al-'adami: it can mean the non-existent, absolute non-existence, relative non-existence compounded with an existential (like blindness), or non-compounded non-existence (like non-acceptance of sharing), or what enters its concept non-existence. Al-wujudi is opposite: it can mean the existent, absolute existence, relative existence, or what does not enter its concept non-existence. He stresses that consideration is of meaning, not wording, giving the example of blindness as 'adami and non-blindness as wujudi.

He then cites al-Mawaqif, defining al-wujudi as that whose affirmation for its subject is by its existence for it externally, like blackness, not like imkan (possibility) which is only in the mind. Al-wujudi is more general than the existent, because some wujudi concepts never actually exist but would if they did. He quotes al-Qadi al-Urmawi to clarify that 'wujudi' does not mean eternal existence but a concept that can receive external existence when it subsists in an existent.

Finally, he defines al-i'tibari as that which has no reality except by mental supposition, though its subject is described by it in reality, like imkan. Al-haqiqi is opposite. Applying these definitions, he concludes that the individuating accidents are wujudi, ha-dhiyya is i'tibari, and the distinction of the individual from others, non-acceptance of sharing, and being not other are all 'adami.

التشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'adami simply means the non-existent (al-ma'dum).
Correction: The author clarifies that 'adami can also refer to the absence of a particular thing (like lack of sight) or to a concept that inherently includes non-existence (like non-acceptance of sharing).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a meaning of 'adami?
A) The non-existent
B) The absence of a certain thing
C) A concept that includes non-existence
D) A concept that includes existence

Key Takeaway
The four attribute types (wujudi, 'adami, haqiqi, i'tibari) are defined by their relation to existence and mental supposition; understanding them is essential for analyzing individuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own beliefs, are they grounded in what is haqiqi (real) or are they merely i'tibari (mental constructions)? Are you willing to test them against reality, even if it costs you comfort?
?
You just learned that al-'adami can include the concept of absence. Is there an absence in your life—of patience, gratitude, or trust—that you have mistakenly accepted as merely 'non-existent' rather than as a real lack you must address?
?
If al-tashakhkhus (individuation) is so subtle that scholars debated its definition, how much more carefully should you examine what makes you distinct from others in the eyes of your Creator? Are you content with being a generic 'good person' or do you seek a unique character of faith?

Detailed Meanings of 'Adami and Wujudi and Their Role in Individuation
The author elaborates on the definitions of 'adami, wujudi, i'tibari, and haqiqi, and applies them to individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations of 'adami and wujudi, the distinction between i'tibari and haqiqi, and how they apply to the concept of individuation.

Core Idea
'Adami and wujudi have multiple senses beyond simple existence and non-existence; their precise meanings determine whether individuation is existential, non-existential, or mental.

After the initial definitions, the author delves deeper into the nuances of 'adami and wujudi, citing various schools and scholars. He then concludes by applying these terms to the individuating accidents, 'thisness', and the individual's distinction from others.

1

تنتهي إلى ما يفيد الهذية، والعدمي قد يطلق على المعدوم، وعلى عدم أمر ما، وعلى ما يدخل في مفهومه العدم، والوجودي بخلافه، والحقيقي على ما هو ثابت في نفس الأمر من غير شائبة الفرض والتقدير والاعتباري بخلافه. فبعد تلخيص المراد بالثبوتي والعدمي، وأن التشخص هو تلك العوارض، أو ما يحصل عندها من الهذية، أو كون الفرد بحيث لا يقبل الشركة، أو عدم قبوله لذلك كان الحق جلياً.

تصور الشيء بوجه ما وإن كان كافياً في الحكم عليه في الجملة. لكن خصوصيات الأحكام ربما تستدعي تصورات مخصوصة، لا بد منها في صحة الحكم، فلا بد في تحقيق أن التعين وجودي أو عدمي اعتباري أو غير اعتباري من بيان ما هو المراد من هذه الألفاظ.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and classification of key terms used in kalam to describe attributes and their role in individuation. The author begins by summarizing the meanings of thubuti (positive) and 'adami (non-existential), and then lists the various interpretations of tashakhkhus (individuation): it could be the accidents themselves, the 'thisness' (ha-dhiyya) arising from them, the individual's non-acceptance of sharing, or the absence of such acceptance. These accidents can culminate in ha-dhiyya and prevention of sharing, and are called al-'awarid al-mushakhkhisa (individuating accidents). To obtain the subject of a proposition, we must clarify tashakhkhus; to obtain the predicate, we must clarify al-wujudi, al-'adami, and al-i'tibari.

?

Logic and Technical Terms

Privative, Existential, Relational, and Real Attributes
The author defines privative and existential attributes and distinguishes relational from real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of privative and existential attributes, and the distinction between relational and real attributes in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Attributes are either privative (involving non-existence) or existential (involving existence); the latter may be relational (mental) or real (external).

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational terminology for classifying attributes in kalam. He defines what makes an attribute privative versus existential, and then further distinguishes among existential attributes those that are merely relational (i'tibari) from those that are real (haqiqi). Understanding these categories is essential for later discussions on divine attributes and predication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the multiple 'قيل' (it was said) which indicate different scholarly opinions.
2
Understand the definitions of 'adami
The passage gives several definitions of privative ('adami). Identify the common thread: it involves non-existence in its concept.
Examples: lack of sight (relative), non-reception of partnership (non-composite).
3
Understand the definitions of wujudi
Existential (wujudi) is the opposite. It is the existent, or existence itself, or what does not involve non-existence in its concept.
Emphasis is on meaning, not wording: 'العمى عدمي واللاعدم وجودي' (blindness is privative, non-blindness is existential).
4
Grasp the relational vs. real distinction
The relational (i'tibari) has no realization except in mental supposition, even if its subject is truly qualified. The real (haqiqi) is its opposite.
Possibility (imkan) is given as an example of a relational attribute.
5
Apply the classification to examples
The author concludes that individuating accidents are existential, relational properties are i'tibari, and distinctness/non-reception of partnership are privative.
Try to classify other attributes you know using these categories.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقيل : العدمي المعدوم، وقيل ما يكون عدماً مطلقاً (۱)، أو مضافاً متركباً، مع وجودي كعدم البصر عما من شأنه، أو غير متركب كعدم قبول الشركة، وقيل، ما يدخل في مفهومه العدم ككون الشيء بحيث (۲) لا يقبل الشركة. والوجودي بخلافه، فهو الموجود، أو الوجود مطلقاً، أو مضافاً، أو ما لا يدخل في مفهومه العدم، والعبرة بالمعنى دون اللفظ، حتى إن العمى عدمي، واللاعدم وجودي، وفي المواقف أن الوجودي ما يكون ثبوته لموصوفه بوجوده له ، أي بحسب الخارج . نحو السواد لا أن يكون ذلك باعتبار وجودهما في العقل واتصاف موصوفه به فيه (۳) أي في العقل دون الخارج كالإمكان (٤)، وهو أعم من الموجود، لجواز وجودي لا يعرض له الوجود أبداً، لكنه بحيث إذا ثبت للموصوف، كان ذلك بوجوده له . وهذا معنى ما قال القاضي الأرموي، إذا قلنا لشيء إنه وجودي، لا نعني أنه دائم الوجود ، بل نعني أنه مفهوم يصح أن يعرض له الوجود الخارجي عند قيامه بموجود، وعند قيامه بمعدوم لا يكون له وجود وكأنه يريد الأعم من وجه، وإلا فمن الموجود ما لا يسمى وجودياً كالانسان، وغيره من المفهومات المستقلة، وأما الاعتباري فهو ما لا تحقق له إلا بحسب فرض العقل، وإن كان موصوفه متصفاً به في نفس الأمر كالإمكان، فإن الإنسان متصف به في نفس الأمر، بمعنى أنه بحيث إذا نسبه العقل إلى الوجود يعقل له وصفاً هو الإمكان، ويقابله الحقيقي إذا تقرر هذا فلا خفاء في أن العوارض المشخصة وجودية، والهذية اعتبارية، وتميز الفرد عما عداه وعدم قبوله الشركة وكونه ليس غيره أو لا يقبل الشركة عدمية .
(٤) الإمكان في اللغة : مصدر أمكن إمكاناً والامكان في الشيء عند المتقدمين هو اظهار ما في قوله إلى الفعل. قال ابن سينا والإمكان إما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم وهو الامتناع واما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب الضرورة في العدم والوجود جميعاً. راجع) الاشارات: ٣٤)، فاعتبار الذات وحدها لا يخلو إما أن يكون مقتضياً لوجوب الوجود أو مقتضياً لامتناع الوجود . (راجع النجاة ص ٣٦٧). ونحن نسمي امكان الوجود قوة الوجود (راجع الشفاء ٢ : ٤٧٧) .
It was said: the privative (al-'adami) is the non-existent (al-ma'dum), and it was said: it is what is absolute non-existence, or relative and composite, along with an existential like the lack of sight in that which is capable of it, or non-composite like the non-reception of partnership. And it was said: it is what enters into its concept non-existence, like a thing being such that it does not admit partnership. And the existential (al-wujudi) is the opposite of that: it is the existent (al-mawjud), or absolute existence, or relative existence, or what does not enter into its concept non-existence, and the consideration is by meaning not by wording, so that blindness is privative, and non-privation is existential. And in al-Mawaqif: the existential is that whose affirmation for its subject is by its existence for it, i.e., according to externals, like blackness; not that this is by consideration of their existence in the intellect and the subject's being qualified by it therein, i.e., in the intellect not in externals, like possibility (al-imkan). And it is more general than the existent, because it is permissible that an existential never be attained by existence, but it is such that when it is established for the subject, that is by its existence for it. And this is the meaning of what al-Qadi al-Urmawi said: when we say of something that it is existential, we do not mean that it is eternally existent, but we mean that it is a concept which it is valid for external existence to occur to it when it subsists in an existent, and when it subsists in a non-existent it has no existence, and it is as if he intends the more general in some respect; otherwise, among existents there is what is not called existential, like man and other independent concepts. As for the relational (al-i'tibari), it is that which has no realization except according to a supposition of the intellect, even if its subject is qualified by it in reality, like possibility: for man is qualified by it in reality, meaning that he is such that when the intellect relates him to existence, it conceives for him a description which is possibility. And its opposite is the real (al-haqiqi). When this is established, there is no hiddenness: the individuating accidents are existential, and the relationals are relational, and the distinctness of the individual from what is other than it, and its non-reception of partnership, and its being not other, or its not admitting partnership, are privative.
(4) Possibility (al-imkan) in language is the masdar of 'amkana imkanan, and possibility in a thing according to the early scholars is the manifestation of what is in his statement to action. Ibn Sina said: possibility either means what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence, which is impossibility, or what entails the negation of necessity in both non-existence and existence. Cf. al-Isharat: 34. For the consideration of the essence alone is either necessitating the necessity of existence or necessitating the impossibility of existence. (Cf. al-Najat p. 367). And we call possibility of existence potentiality of existence (Cf. al-Shifa 2:477).
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies attributes into privative ('adami) and existential (wujudi), and then distinguishes between relational (i'tibari) and real (haqiqi) within the existential category. The author begins by presenting various opinions on what 'adami means: some say it is the non-existent, others say it is absolute non-existence, or relative and composite (like lack of sight when sight is expected), or non-composite (like not admitting partnership). The defining characteristic is that non-existence enters into its concept. The existential is its opposite: it is either the existent itself, or absolute or relative existence, or what does not involve non-existence in its concept. The key point is that the classification depends on meaning, not wording—e.g., blindness is privative even though it is described as a 'lack' of sight, while non-privation is existential. The author then cites al-Mawaqif to clarify that an existential attribute is one whose affirmation for its subject is due to its existence externally (like blackness), not merely in the intellect. Possibility (imkan) is an example of something existential that is more general than the existent, because it can be predicated even if it never actually exists, but if it does exist, it does so externally. This is in line with al-Qadi al-Urmawi's view that 'existential' does not mean always existing, but means a concept that can receive external existence when attached to an existent. Then the relational (i'tibari) is defined as something that has no realization except by mental supposition, even though its subject is truly qualified by it in reality—again using possibility as an example. The real (haqiqi) is its opposite. From this, it becomes clear that individuating accidents (like specific characteristics) are existential, while relational properties are i'tibari. Furthermore, things like an individual's distinctness from others, not admitting partnership, and being not-other are all privative attributes. The footnote on imkan elaborates on its linguistic and technical definition, citing Ibn Sina and distinguishing two meanings: one that entails negation of necessity of non-existence (impossibility) and one that entails negation of necessity of both existence and non-existence.

Key Terms
العدمي
الوجودي
الاعتباري
الحقيقي
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'adami attributes are simply non-existent things, but they are real descriptions like blindness.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'adami attributes involve non-existence in their concept, but they are still meaningful predicates applied to subjects (e.g., blindness is a real quality of a person).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a relational (i'tibari) attribute from a real (haqiqi) attribute?
A) The relational is always privative.
B) The relational has no realization except in mental supposition, even if its subject is truly qualified.
C) The relational is always existential.
D) The relational depends on external existence.

Key Takeaway
Privative attributes involve non-existence in their concept, while existential attributes involve existence; among the latter, relational attributes are mentally conceived but apply to real subjects, like possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your character is made of attributes. Which of your flaws are privative—like the absence of patience—and which are existential—like pride? How does this distinction change how you seek to change them, if at all?
?
The lesson says relational attributes depend on mental supposition yet are real in themselves. Consider your most deeply held belief about your own identity. Is it a real (haqiqi) attribute grounded in external reality, or a relational (i'tibari) one sustained only by your thoughts? What would happen if you stopped mentally affirming it?
?
Possibility (imkan) is a relational attribute that means 'could be otherwise'. In your relationship with Allah, have you reduced obedience to a mere possibility—something that could happen but doesn't? What would it take for your faith to become a real (haqiqi) attribute in your life?

Why Combining Universals Does Not Yield Particularity
Author explains and defends why two universals cannot combine to form a particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why combining two universal concepts cannot produce a particular concept, and how the author refutes an objection.

Core Idea
Two universals combined remain universal, never particular.

In this passage, the author deepens the earlier point about determination by clarifying a potential confusion. If universality means the mind can apply a concept to many, then combining two such concepts cannot suddenly restrict it to one. The author addresses a clever objection and shows why the composite remains universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فإن قيل' and 'قلنا'.
2
Understand the condition for particularity
Identify what makes a concept particular: the mind cannot suppose it true of many. This is 'imtina' al-shirkah' (impossibility of sharing).
This is the key criterion.
3
Follow why combining universals fails
The author states that each element (combined, combined-to, combination) is universal, so the composite is mentally shareable. Even if only one external instance exists, the concept remains universal.
Example: the concept of the Necessary (God) is universal in thought.
4
Analyze the objection and the responses
The objection says the whole might differ from its parts. The author gives two responses: first, that describing a universal with another universal does not make it particular; second, that the claim is about necessity, not possibility.
The second response acknowledges that a composite might coincidentally be particular, but it is not entailed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد في التعين من كون المفهوم بحيث لا يمكن للعقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين، وهذا معنى امتناع الشركة ذهناً، ومعلوم أنه لا يحصل بانضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، لأن كلاً من المنضم والمنضم إليه والانضمام لكونه كلياً، يمكن للعقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين، بل على ما لا يتناهى من الأفراد، وإن كان بحسب الخارج ربما لا يوجد منه الأفراد، بل يمتنع تعدده كمفهوم الواجب. فإن قيل: حكم الكلي قد تخالف حكم كل واحد، فيجوز أن يكون كل من المنضم والمنضم إليه كلياً، والمجموع جزئياً. قلنا: لا معنى للانضمام ههنا سوى أن العقل يعتبر مفهوماً كلياً كالإنسان، ثم يعتبر له وصفاً كلياً كالفاضل ومعلوم بالضرورة أن الكلي الموصوف بالأوصاف الكلية لا ينتهي إلى حد الهذية، حتى لو كان ذلك الوصف هو مفهوم الجزئية والتشخص وامتناع قبول الشركة، كانت الكلية بحالها. وقد يجاب: بأن المراد أن انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، وتقييده به لا يستلزم الجزئية والتشخص، وإن كان قد يفيدها، فيكون حاصل الكلام أن المركبات العقلية مثل الجوهر المتميز والجسم النامي، والحيوان الناطق، والإنسان الفاضل، لا يلزم أن يكون جزئية، بل قد يكون كلية، وهذا من الوضوح بحيث لا ينبغي أن يخير به فضلا عن أن يجعل من المطالب العلمية.
It is necessary in determination that the concept be such that the mind cannot suppose it true of many, and this is the meaning of impossibility of sharing mentally. And it is known that this is not obtained by combining the universal with the universal, because each of the combined, the combined-to, and the combination, being universal, can be supposed by the mind to be true of many, indeed of infinite individuals, even if externally perhaps no individuals of it exist, but rather its multiplicity is impossible like the concept of the Necessary. If it is said: the judgment of the universal may differ from the judgment of each individual, so it is possible that each of the combined and the combined-to is universal, and the whole is particular. We say: there is no meaning for combination here except that the mind considers a universal concept like 'human', then considers for it a universal attribute like 'virtuous', and it is known necessarily that the universal described by universal attributes does not reach the level of particularity, even if that attribute is the concept of particularity, individuation, and impossibility of accepting sharing, the universality remains as it is. And it may be answered: that what is meant is that the combining of a universal to a universal, and its restriction by it, does not necessitate particularity and individuation, even if it may sometimes yield it. Thus the upshot is that mental composites such as 'discriminating substance', 'growing body', 'rational animal', and 'virtuous human' are not necessarily particular, but may be universal. And this is so obvious that it should not be used as a point of hesitation, let alone made one of the scientific inquiries.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation and defense of the earlier claim that combining universals does not produce a particular. The author reiterates the condition for determination: the concept must be such that the mind cannot suppose it true of many individuals – that is 'imtina' al-shirkah dhihnan'. He then explains why combining two universals fails: each component (the combined, the combined-to, and the act of combination) is itself universal, so the mind can still suppose the whole composite true of many individuals, even if in external reality only one individual exists (as with the concept of God). An objection is raised: perhaps the judgment of the whole differs from its parts; the parts are universal but the whole might be particular. The author responds by analyzing what 'combination' means here: it is merely the mind considering one universal concept and then adding another universal descriptor. The resulting composite remains universal, because describing a universal with another universal does not make it particular – even if the descriptor is 'particularity' itself. Then an alternative answer is given: the intended point is that combining universals does not entail particularity, though it might sometimes yield it in specific cases. The author illustrates with examples of mental composites (e.g., 'rational animal', 'virtuous human') that can still be predicated of many. He concludes that this is so obvious it should not be a matter of debate.

Key Terms
امتناع الشركة (imtina' al-shirkah)
الكلي (al-kullī)
الجزئي (al-juz'ī)
المركبات العقلية (al-murakkabāt al-'aqliyya)
الهذية (al-hudhiyya?)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a concept has only one external instance, it must be a particular concept.
Correction: A concept can be universal in the mind (sharable across many possible instances) even if only one instance exists externally, like the concept of the Necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that combining two universals can yield a particular concept?
A) The combination is not a real mental operation.
B) Each component is universal, so the whole remains mentally shareable.
C) Universals cannot be combined at all.
D) The objection is false because the parts are not truly universal.

Key Takeaway
Combining two universals always yields a universal, not a particular, because each component and the combination itself are universally applicable in thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by combining labels like 'Muslim', 'student', 'activist'—thinking these make you unique. But the author teaches that all such combinations remain universal. Your true uniqueness before Allah is not in these composites but in your individual soul that only Allah knows. How can you shift your focus from seeking identity in universals to seeking the particular relationship Allah wants with you?
?
The lesson says that even the concept of 'particularity' itself is universal. Have you ever believed that your trials or blessings are so uniquely yours that no one else can truly understand? Yet the concept of struggle itself is universal. How can this truth push you to seek solace in the community of believers and to see your experiences as a shared part of the human and Muslim condition?
