Mu'tazilite Classification and the Author's Correction
The author critiques the Mu'tazilite classification and offers a corrected division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite threefold division of the known and the author's critique leading to a revised classification.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites wrongly made thabit a subset of ma'dum; the author shows a more nuanced relationship.

After establishing the basic terms, the author presents the Mu'tazilite position that introduces a state (hal) as an intermediary between existent and non-existent, and divides non-existent into established and negated. He then critiques this by showing that established is not a subset of non-existent but rather overlaps with existent and state.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite division
They classify the known into mawjud, hal, and ma'dum; and within ma'dum, thabit and manfi. Note the role of hal as an intermediary.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author points out that the apparent wording makes thabit a division of ma'dum, but this is incorrect. Thabit includes mawjud and hal as well.
There is a general-specific relation (عموم من وجه) between thabit and ma'dum.
3
Examine the revised division
The author proposes a new order: first check if the known is realized (mutahaqqiq) in itself, then consider if it has external being.
This avoids the confusion of making thabit a subset of ma'dum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعيان فله تقرر في نفسه من غير عكس، فيكون الموجود أخص من الثابت. وكل ما لا تقرر له في نفسه لا كون له في الأعيان وليس كل ما لا كون له تقرر له، فيكون المنفي أخص من المعدوم، فيكون بعض المعدوم لا منفياً بل ثابتاً، ومنهم من خالف في الأمرين جميعاً وهم بعض المعتزلة قالوا : - المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فإن كان له (۱) ذلك بالاستقلال فهو الموجود، وإن كان بتبعية الغير فهو الحال، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فهو المعدوم، والمعدوم إن كان متحققاً في نفسه فثابت، وإلا فمنفي. فقد جعلوا بعض المعدوم ثابتاً. وأثبتوا بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة هو الحال، وظاهر العبارة يوهم أن الثابت قسم من المعدوم ، وليس كذلك. بل بينهما عموم من وجه لأنه يشمل الموجود والحال بخلاف المعدوم والمعدوم يشمل المنفي بخلاف الثابت وان كان المعدوم مبايناً للمنفي على ما صرح به في تلخيص المحصل (۲) من أن القائلين بكون المعدوم شيئاً لا يقولون للممتنع معدوم بل منفي . كان الأول في هذا التقسيم أن يقال : المعلوم إن لم يتحقق في نفسه فمنفي، وإن تحقق فإن كان له كون في الأعيان، فإما بالاستقلال فموجود، أو بالتبيعة فحال ، وإن لم يكن له (۳) كون في الأعيان فمعدوم، وفي التقسيم السابق أنه إن لم يتحقق فمنفي، وان تحقق فثابت، وحينئذ إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود، وإلا فمعدوم.
The existing is that which has determination (taqarrur) in itself without reciprocal, so the existent (al-mawjud) is more specific than the established (al-thabit). Everything that has no determination in itself has no being (kawn) in external entities, and not everything that has no being has determination, so the negated (al-manfi) is more specific than the non-existent (al-ma'dum). Thus some of the non-existent is not negated but rather established. Some of them (i.e., some of the Mu'tazila) opposed both matters and said: the known (al-ma'lum) if it has being in external entities: if it has that independently then it is the existent; if by following another then it is the state (al-hal); and if it does not have being in external entities then it is the non-existent. The non-existent if it is realized (mutahqaq) in itself then it is established, otherwise it is negated. So they made some of the non-existent established. And they posited between the existent and non-existent an intermediary which is the state. The apparent wording suggests that the established is a division of the non-existent, but it is not. Rather, there is a general-specific relation from one aspect, because it includes the existent and the state unlike the non-existent, and the non-existent includes the negated unlike the established. And if the non-existent is distinct from the negated as clarified in Talkhis al-Muhassal, namely that those who say that the non-existent is a thing do not call the impossible (mumtani') non-existent but rather negated. Then the proper procedure in this division is to say: the known if it is not realized in itself then it is negated; and if it is realized, then if it has being in external entities, either independently so it is existent, or dependently so it is state; and if it does not have being in external entities then it is non-existent. In the previous division it was that if it is not realized then it is negated, and if realized then it is established, and then if it has being in external entities then it is existent, otherwise it is non-existent.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a classification and analysis of the categories of the known (al-ma'lum). The author begins by establishing that the existent (al-mawjud) is more specific than the established (al-thabit), because the existent has determination (taqarrur) in itself, while the established may include what is not external. Then he notes that the negated (al-manfi) is more specific than the non-existent (al-ma'dum), since some non-existent things can be established in the mind. The Mu'tazila introduced a threefold classification: if the known has being in external entities independently, it is existent; dependently, it is a state (hal); if no external being, it is non-existent. Within non-existent, they distinguish the established (mutahqaq) from the negated. The author critiques this by showing that the relation between established and non-existent is not simple subordination; rather, there is a general-specific relation from one aspect. He then proposes a revised division: first, if the known is not realized in itself, it is negated; if realized, then if it has external being, it is either existent (independent) or state (dependent); if no external being, it is non-existent. This revised division avoids the confusion of making the established a subset of non-existent.

Key Terms
حال
كون
متحقق
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thabit is exclusively a division of ma'dum (non-existent).
Correction: Thabit includes existent (mawjud) and state (hal) as well, so it is not a subset of ma'dum.

Checkpoint
According to the author's revised division, what is the first criterion for classifying the known?
A) Whether it has being in external entities.
B) Whether it is realized in itself.
C) Whether it is independent or dependent.
D) Whether it is existent or non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The proper classification of the known begins with realization (mutahaqqiq), then external being (kawn), avoiding the error of making thabit a subset of ma'dum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you clung to a classification system without examining its logical flaws, and what was the consequence?
?
How does this meticulous analysis of 'the known' challenge you to be more precise in your own understanding of theological categories?
?
In what area of your faith do you need to revisit foundational definitions to avoid category mistakes?

Second Proof: Non-Existence as Negating Attribute and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents a proof and its refutation regarding non-existence as a negating attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof regarding non-existence as a negating attribute and its refutation by distinguishing between a negating attribute and a negated attribute.

Core Idea
A negating attribute cannot inhere; a negated attribute does not negate the subject.

This lesson continues Sanusi's analysis of the attributes of non-existence. The second proof attempts to show that non-existence cannot be an attribute because it would negate its subject. The refutation cleverly distinguishes two senses of 'negating attribute.' Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and refutation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise logical structure: a proof followed by a refutation.
The passage is very short, so take time to understand each phrase.
2
Identify the two interpretations
In the refutation, Sanusi presents two possible meanings of 'negating attribute.' Find both in the Arabic and translation. What is the difference between 'attribute that is negation' and 'negated attribute'?
This distinction is the key to understanding why the proof fails.
3
Understand why the proof fails
For each interpretation, explain why the proof's conclusion does not follow. If 'negating attribute' means a concept that negates, it cannot inhere in a subject. If it means an attribute that is negated, then the subject is not necessarily negated.
The refutation does not affirm non-existence as an attribute; it only shows the proof is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن العدم صفة نفي فينتفي الموصوف به.
والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة (٢) هي نفي أو إن أريد صفة منفية .
Second: that non-existence is a negating attribute, so the subject described by it is negated. And the second [refutation] is by denying the first if what is meant is an attribute that is negation, or if what is meant is a negated attribute.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This lesson gives the second proof and its refutation. The proof argues that non-existence ('adam) is a negating attribute (sifat nafi), so if it were to inhere in a subject, the subject would be negated. The refutation denies the premise by distinguishing two senses: if by 'attribute' is meant a negation (i.e., a concept that negates), then it cannot inhere; if it is meant a negated attribute (i.e., an attribute that is itself negated), then the proof fails.

Key Terms
العدم
صفة نفي
الموصوف
المنفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the refutation of the second proof work?
A) By affirming that non-existence is a negated attribute.
B) By denying that non-existence is an attribute.
C) By distinguishing between a negating attribute and a negated attribute.
D) By stating that the subject is always existent.

Key Takeaway
The second proof against non-existence as an attribute fails because the term 'negating attribute' is ambiguous; the refutation shows that either it cannot inhere or it does not entail subject negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever fall into the trap of treating negations (like 'He is not unjust') as if they were positive qualities? How might this affect your worship and trust in Him? Commit to examining one attribute of God this week and consciously separating what it affirms from what it denies.
?
This lesson shows that a subtle ambiguity in a term can invalidate an entire argument. Where in your own understanding of faith have you held onto a proof or reasoning that was actually based on an unclear definition? Will you sincerely search for such ambiguities and correct them, even if it humbles you?
?
The refutation here relies on precise language. Are you willing to invest the effort to learn the precise meanings of key Arabic terms in your aqeedah, so that you are not misled by vague understandings? What specific term will you start with today?

The Three Objections Against Thubut al-Ma'dum
Taftazani enumerates three objections (third, fourth, fifth) against thubut al-ma'dum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific objections raised against the theory of thubut al-ma'dum (the stable existence of non-existent things) and why they seem to lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
The three objections attack thubut al-ma'dum by showing it leads to multiplicity of gods, infinity paradoxes, and logical inconsistency.

Taftazani continues his examination of thubut al-ma'dum by presenting three powerful objections. These objections are the third, fourth, and fifth in a series, each targeting a crucial aspect: the independence of thubut, the finitude of the infinite, and the logical relationship between the non-existent and the negated. Understanding these objections is essential to grasp the dialectical context of the refutations that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections in Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the numbered structure: third, fourth, fifth.
2
Identify the structure of each objection
Each objection presents a premise and a conclusion. Work through each one: what is the assumption, and what contradiction does it yield?
The third objection uses independence, the fourth uses infinity, the fifth uses logical classification.
3
Note key terms
Identify the Arabic terms: thubut, al-ma'dum, al-manfi, al-wajib. Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are central to the entire debate.
4
Reflect on why these are serious challenges
Think about why these objections would be considered strong arguments against the view that non-existent things are somehow 'established'.
The refutations in the next lesson will address these points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن ثبوت الذوات عندكم ليس من الغير فيلزم تعدد الواجب . الرابع : أنها غير متناهية مع أن الموجود منها متناه. فالكل أكثر من الباقية على العدم بمنتاه فتكون متناهية . الخامس : أن المعدوم كان مساوياً للمنفي أو أخص منه لم يكن ثابتاً، وإن كان أعم منه لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، والا لما بقي فرق بين العام والخاص. بل ثابتاً . وهو صادق على المنفي فيلزم ثبوته وهو محال . ورد الأول : بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة فإن قيل : هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود. أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود. والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة هي نفي أو إن أريد صفة منفية . والثالث : بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته . والرابع : يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه . بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف. والخامس : بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرفاً بل قد وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي . فإن قيل : المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات .
The third: That the essences' being established (thubut) according to you is not from another, so it necessitates multiplicity of the Necessary (al-wajib). The fourth: That they are infinite while the existent among them is finite. So the whole is more than what remains in non-existence by a finite amount, so they are finite. The fifth: That the non-existent (al-ma'dum)—if it were equal to the negated (al-manfi) or more specific than it, it would not be established (thabit); and if it were more general than it, it would not be pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between the general and the specific. Rather, it is established. And it is true of the negated, so its thubut is necessary, which is impossible. And the first [objection] was replied: By the permissibility that the attribute of the essence (al-dhat) with existence (al-wujud) may be temporally originated (hadith) by power (al-qudra). For if it is said: It is negated, otherwise there would be regression (tasalsul) and the attribute of the non-existent with existence. It was answered by denying the impossibility of regression in the established (al-thabit) and the attribute of the established with existence. And the second [objection] was replied: By denying the first if what is meant is a quality that is negation, or if what is meant is a negated quality. And the third [objection] was replied: That the Necessary (al-wajib) is what is independent of another in its existence, not in its thubut. And the fourth [objection] was replied: It denies the finitude of what exceeds another by a finite amount. Rather, if the other is finite, proving that by applying it between it and the whole is weak. And the fifth [objection] was replied: That its not being pure negation does not necessitate its being pure affirmation; rather, it may [be both or neither], so only some of the non-existent is [said to be] established, so the thubut of the negated is not necessary. For if it is said: What is meant is that if it were more general, it would be distinguished from the specific, so it is established as an implication (ilzaman). We say: Then many of the premises are invalid.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage presents three objections (the third, fourth, and fifth) against the view that non-existent things (al-ma'dum) have a kind of stable existence (thubut), and then gives the refutations (rudud) to these objections as well as to the first and second objections (which were presumably stated earlier). The argument is set within the kalām debate over whether non-existent quiddities are 'established' (thabit) prior to creation. The third objection argues that if quiddities were established independently (not from another), they would share the attribute of self-sufficiency with the Necessary Existent, leading to a multiplicity of necessary beings—a clear impossibility. The fourth objection notes that if non-existent quiddities are infinite (since there are infinitely many possible things), but only a finite number actually exist, then the whole set would be only finitely more than the existing ones, implying finitude—a contradiction. The fifth objection uses logical relationships: if the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is either equal to or more specific than the negated (al-manfi), it would not be established; if more general, it would not be pure negation, and thus would be established, which when applied to the negated would entail thubut of the negated—also impossible. The reply (rada) to the first objection (which dealt with the temporality of existence) allows that the attribute of the essence with existence may be temporally originated by divine power, and denies the impossibility of regression (tasalsul) in matters of stability (thubut) rather than existence. The reply to the second objection denies the premise if 'attribute' means negation or a negated quality. The reply to the third clarifies that the Necessary Existent is independent in its existence, not in its thubut, so the threat of multiplicity is avoided. The reply to the fourth denies that the finite comparison is valid, as proving finitude through a direct mapping is weak. The reply to the fifth explains that not being pure negation does not entail pure affirmation; only some aspect of the non-existent may be established, so the thubut of the negated does not follow. A final objection (fa-in qīla) is raised: if the non-existent were more general than the negated, it would be distinguished and thus established as an implication. The answer (qulnā) rejects this by stating that then many premises would be invalid.

Key Terms
thubut
al-ma'dum
al-manfi
al-wajib

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objections are independent and unrelated.
Correction: They are part of a systematic attack on thubut, addressing self-sufficiency, infinitude, and logical classification.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth objection argue about the finitude of non-existent things?
A) They are finite because only finite exist
B) They are infinite but must be finite due to comparison with the finite existent
C) They are both finite and infinite
D) The argument is inconclusive

Key Takeaway
The three objections use arguments from multiplicity, infinitude, and logical classification to challenge thubut al-ma'dum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The third objection warns that treating essences as self-sufficient leads to multiplicity of the Necessary. In your understanding of God's unity, do you ever implicitly attribute independent reality to His attributes or actions? How can you guard your tawhid from such hidden shirk?
?
The fourth objection uses the tension between infinite non-existents and finite existents. When you try to reason about Allah's creation, do you sometimes fall into assuming human logic can fully capture His omnipotence? What step can you take today to submit your intellect to revelation more humbly?
